The Mag Seven Is Cracking: Winners, Losers & What Comes Next

RiskReversal Media(131.0K) ·34:44 ·6.2K views · 2026-04-08
Dan Nathan (RiskReversal Media) Gene Munster (Deepwater Asset Management)
The "Magnificent Seven" trade is bifurcating as investors shift focus from pure infrastructure builds to the actual utility and ROI of AI, leaving laggards like Microsoft and Apple under pressure to deliver "Google-level" breakthroughs to justify their valuations.
TL;DR The "Magnificent Seven" trade is bifurcating as investors shift focus from pure infrastructure builds to the actual utility and ROI of AI, leaving laggards like Microsoft and Apple under pressure to deliver "Google-level" breakthroughs to justify their valuations. ...

TL;DR

The "Magnificent Seven" trade is bifurcating as investors shift focus from pure infrastructure builds to the actual utility and ROI of AI, leaving laggards like Microsoft and Apple under pressure to deliver "Google-level" breakthroughs to justify their valuations.

AI Infrastructure: The Hyperscaler CAPEX Reality

  • Anthropic’s Massive Scale: Anthropic’s revenue run rate has surged from $9 billion to $30 billion in a matter of months, validating the massive demand for compute capacity.
  • Broadcom & Google Synergy: The partnership between Broadcom and Google on Tensor Processing Units (TPUs) is a major differentiator for Google Cloud (GCP), allowing them to offer specialized hardware that challenges Nvidia’s dominance.
  • The CAPEX Cliff: While hyperscalers (Amazon, Google, Meta, Microsoft) saw 60% CAPEX growth recently, projections for 2027 suggest a slowdown to ~20%.
      • Market Psychology: Investors are growing wary of whether these companies can maintain a 35-40% growth rate if their primary customers (the big four) slow their infrastructure spending.
  • Nvidia Sentiment: Despite stellar earnings and 70% expected EPS growth, Nvidia's stock has shown recent weakness. Deepwater has reduced its position by half, citing a "psychological shift" regarding how long hyperscalers can sustain record-breaking spend.

The Utility Gap: Microsoft and Apple’s "Identity Crisis"

  • Microsoft’s Copilot Struggles: Sentiment on Microsoft is turning negative as early AI enthusiasm meets reality.
      • Low Utility: Gene Munster notes that Copilot has yet to become a "must-use" tool for most professionals, describing current iterations as disappointing in Excel and PowerPoint.
      • The "Google Moment": Microsoft needs a definitive AI breakthrough similar to Google’s recent model successes to regain momentum.
  • Apple’s Personalized AI: Apple is pivoting toward "Personalized AI" with Siri as the focal point, emphasizing data privacy.
      • Supercycle Potential: A hardware device that runs advanced AI locally (safely) could trigger a massive upgrade cycle for "normies" who find current AI tools too complex.
      • Geopolitical Risk: Apple remains the most vulnerable megacap to US-China tensions and potential conflict in Taiwan.

The Future of the Elon Musk Ecosystem

  • SpaceX IPO Dynamics: SpaceX has filed confidentially for an IPO with a potential $2 trillion valuation.
      • Retail Access: Musk intends to reserve ~30% of the IPO for retail investors, potentially including Tesla shareholders, to maintain his "retail-friendly" brand.
      • Efficiency: While SpaceX doesn't *need* public money (given its ability to raise billions privately), being public provides a cheaper, more efficient vehicle for Musk’s long-term Mars vision.
  • Tesla vs. SpaceX: Tesla has traded poorly since the SpaceX filing, leading to fears of "investor cannibalization."
      • The Merger Theory: There is growing speculation that Tesla and SpaceX could eventually merge, combining Tesla’s robotics/AI with SpaceX’s launch capabilities into a single "Master Plan" entity.

The Changing Workforce and "Intelligent Alpha"

  • Knowledge Work Reductions: A trend is emerging where companies announce AI investments while simultaneously cutting staff by ~15% (e.g., Pinterest, Morgan Stanley, Oracle).
  • The Practitioner Shift: Asset managers must evolve from merely *using* AI tools to becoming practitioners who optimize them.
  • Token Pricing Paradox: While the *cost* of tokens is dropping 10x per year, the total *spend* for companies seeking the "most advanced" intelligence is actually increasing as they chase high-end performance.

***

Notable Quotes

"We are still in the second inning [of AI]... It doesn't happen going from $9 to $30 billion [revenue] over a four-month period unless something bigger is going on." — Gene Munster
"Microsoft needs to have a 'Google moment'... they’re really plagued by two headwinds: seat growth uncertainty for knowledge workers, and the fact that they haven't done something truly exciting in AI yet." — Gene Munster
"If you're in the camp that AI is just going to have an 'impact,' you should probably continue to own these stocks. But for the valuations to hold, it has to have a massive impact." — Dan Nathan
"I have a belief that I might be out of a job in two years... we have to face the reality that machines could take our job." — Gene Munster

BREAKING NEWS: JD Vance Holds Event In Hungary After U.S. And Iran Agree To 2-Week Ceasefire

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·38:21 ·6.1K views · 2026-04-08
JD Vance (Vice President of the United States)
Vice President JD Vance’s visit to Budapest underscores a burgeoning "conservative international" alliance, where he defended Hungarian sovereignty against EU pressure and announced a "fragile" 12-hour-old ceasefire agreement with Iran.
TL;DR Vice President JD Vance’s visit to Budapest underscores a burgeoning "conservative international" alliance, where he defended Hungarian sovereignty against EU pressure and announced a "fragile" 12-hour-old ceasefire agreement with Iran. Ceasefire Terms: VP Vance announced...

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance’s visit to Budapest underscores a burgeoning "conservative international" alliance, where he defended Hungarian sovereignty against EU pressure and announced a "fragile" 12-hour-old ceasefire agreement with Iran.

Breakthrough in the Middle East: Iran Ceasefire

  • Ceasefire Terms: VP Vance announced a tentative truce reached with Tehran approximately 8-12 hours prior to his speech.
      • Strategic Concessions: Iran has agreed to open the Straits of Hormuz and cease threats to the global economy. In exchange, the U.S. and its allies have agreed to halt active attacks.
      • Military Objectives: Vance asserted that the U.S. successfully achieved its goal of decimating Iran’s conventional military capabilities before entering negotiations.
  • Diplomatic Skepticism: Vance characterized the truce as "fragile," noting internal divisions within the Iranian government.
      • While the Iranian Foreign Minister signals a willingness to negotiate, Vance accused other factions within the Iranian system of lying about the nature of the military damage and the agreement terms.
      • Economic Leverage: The VP warned that if Iran "cheats," the U.S. maintains "extraordinary economic leverage" and an "impatient" President ready to resume pressure.

National Sovereignty and Foreign Influence

  • Defining Influence: Vance pushed back against critics who labeled his visit as "foreign influence," arguing that true interference comes from transnational organizations.
      • Criticism of Brussels: He condemned the European Union for withholding billions in funding to pressure Hungary on border policy and education reform.
      • Ukrainian Pressure: Vance described Ukrainian threats to shut down pipelines or target Hungarian officials as "scandalous" and an "assault on sovereignty."
  • Support for Viktor Orbán: Vance praised Prime Minister Orbán as a "partner for peace," specifically noting Orbán’s ability to understand both Russian and Ukrainian positions—a prerequisite he deems necessary for a peaceful settlement.
  • The Symbolism of Sovereignty: Vance referenced the Crown of Saint Stephen in the Hungarian Parliament as a reminder that power belongs to the Hungarian people, not "Brussels bureaucrats."

Reforming the University Model

  • Institutional Mission: Balázs Orbán (Chairman, MCC) framed the Mathias Corvinus Collegium as a "mission" to educate patriotic Hungarians capable of international competition while remaining rooted in national values.
  • Vance on Educational Ideology: The VP praised Hungary for imposing "democratic constraints" on education funding.
      • Defunding Ideology: He argued that taxpayers should not be forced to fund "radical gender ideology" or "civil unrest" in academic settings.
      • US Policy Shifts: Vance highlighted recent U.S. efforts to end race-based discrimination in admissions and promote a "meritocracy" that draws students from diverse geographic backgrounds rather than just "insular" elite social networks.
  • The Role of the Academy: Vance argued that universities should serve two primary functions: providing "useful skills" for the workforce and fostering the ability to "think critically" to participate in democracy.

Geopolitics and Elite Social Networks

  • Energy Policy Critiques: Vance criticized European leadership for creating an energy crisis by becoming dependent on Russia while simultaneously labeling Russia a security threat. He contrasted this with U.S. policy aimed at "energy dominance."
  • The "Insular" Elite: Reflecting on his time at Yale Law School, Vance noted that political centers like D.C. and Brussels have become self-reinforcing networks that are disconnected from "normal" citizens.
  • Advice to Students: He urged young conservatives to avoid "blackpilling" (cynical withdrawal from politics).
      • Persistence: He emphasized that "civilization was not built overnight" and requires long-term institutional building rather than instant gratification.

Notable Quotes

"Foreign influence is when other governments threaten, cajole, and try to use economic influence to tell you how to vote... That is fundamentally an assault on your sovereignty." — JD Vance
"The Iranians are better negotiators than they are fighters... if they negotiate in good faith, we will be able to find a deal, but that’s a big 'if'." — JD Vance
"Taxpayer funding is not unlimited... You can say whatever you want, but in Hungary, you don’t get unlimited subsidy if you want to say something that’s really crazy or that promotes violence." — JD Vance
"Blackpilling is how you give power to the forces that are trying to destroy what our ancestors built. We need to take power back from those people and build the kind of institutions that can actually save our society." — JD Vance
Mr. Vice President, ladies and gentlemen, dear friends of MCC, a warm welcome to all of you on this special occasion. It's an honor to welcome Vice President of the United States JD Vance to the Mathias Corvinus Collegium. My name is Balázs Orbán; I'm the chairman of the board of MCC. I remember that almost exactly three years ago, in the summer of 2021, we began discussions with then-Senator Vance's team about how great it would be to host him at MCC. At that time, we hoped he could join us to talk about his book, Hillbilly Elegy, and discuss his role in shaping modern conservative ideas in the United States. Since then, much has changed. First of all, his second book is on the horizon, to be published in a few months. And since then, he has become Vice President of the United States. His visit to Budapest is especially meaningful to us. I can confidently say that the friendship between Hungary and the United States, the strong bond between our two sovereign nations, has never been better. We are very grateful that you, Mr. Vice President, came to Budapest and for the inspiring speech you gave yesterday. And we are particularly grateful to you that you found time to visit MCC. Because MCC is not just an institution, it's a mission. It is a mission to lay the foundation of Hungary's future success by educating the next generation of patriotic Hungarians. Young men and women who are committed to their country and at the same time fully capable of competing on an international stage. MCC also stands as a cornerstone in the renewal of Hungary's university model. As a foundation-based institution, it is built on the belief that true talent development requires more than knowledge alone. It requires values, and it requires a clear worldview. We openly affirm that the values we represent are rooted in the commitment to our nation. We would like to teach our students that their talent matters most when it is placed in the service of their community and their country. As you can imagine, this approach has not been without its critics. Liberal voices around the world continue to challenge us, and the reform of Hungary's university model has become a subject of political debate. In fact, progressive forces in Brussels have taken steps that have sanctioned Hungarian students simply because Hungary chose to reshape its higher education system to better meet the challenges of our time. In 2024, they even tried to silence conservative voices by trying to shut down the NatCon conference in Brussels that we organized together with the Edmund Burke Foundation. Quite a hostile environment. But in those moments, there were voices of support. One of the most notable among them was and is Vice President Vance. He has often spoken critically about the direction many modern universities are taking. The concerns he raises are very similar to ours. In many parts of the Western world, universities are increasingly shaped by strong progressive ideology—one that can limit freedom of thought, restrict open debate, and weaken both scientific research and true talent development. So, Mr. Vice President, allow me to thank you, not only for being with us today, but for standing with us when it truly matters. Your support helps us defend our position with credibility and conviction. To us, this is more than a gesture; it's a clear sign of the enduring strength of American-Hungarian relations. The Mathias Corvinus Collegium offers programs, courses, and opportunities to more than 8,000 students in over 30 locations across Europe. What began as a mainly Hungarian initiative has grown far beyond that. We are expanding across the region and internationally, with a presence in cities like Brussels, Berlin, and Vienna. MCC stands out because it combines education, research, and public debate. We are both an educational institution and a think tank. We publish books, host conferences, and bring together students, scholars, and international experts to exchange ideas. MCC's success has definitely not gone unnoticed. And the best proof of our work is here today: the many talented, thoughtful, and inspiring students who are part of this wonderful community. Mr. Vice President, thank you very much once again for honoring us with your presence today. We are truly delighted to welcome you at MCC. And now, we will have a moderated discussion hosted by Zoltán Szalai, Director General of MCC. With that, I invite to the floor the Vice President of the United States, JD Vance. Good morning, everyone. Good morning, thank you. It's good to be with you. Good morning, Mr. Vice President. Thank you very much for coming to MCC. We have a big audience today and we would like to have more time, but we have only 30 minutes, so I'll start immediately. Sure. Why did you accept our invitation to MCC? It's the first time that the US Vice President is coming to Hungary, that you are here and you are immediately at MCC on stage with a conversation with us. Of course, as you know, MCC is an institution where we have more than 8,000 students in 30 centers all across the country and in the neighboring countries. We also do a lot of research, a lot of events, and from the age of 10, basically until the PhD, until the end of the PhD studies, we have a lot of students. Why was it for you important to come to us? Well, I'll give you two answers. So the first is that I was having lunch with Viktor Orbán yesterday and I said, "What can I do to help?" and he said, "Go and speak at MCC," and I said, "Okay." Teach me ever to ask that question again. But no, we're very excited to be here because it is such an important institution. And what I like about MCC is that it shows—I mean I believe this was started in 1996, correct? And what I like is that educational institutions... there's this attitude that certain people have that they should be completely distinct from all democratic influence, from the sovereign will of the people. The idea that the university or the educational institutions stand completely apart and should just basically be able to receive unlimited funding from the taxpayer even though they don't support the civilization that they were founded on, should receive unlimited resources and unlimited public support even when they go at the very people that they ask that support from. And I think that what you guys are showing is that, you know, academic institutions are part of the civilization that gives birth to them. They're part of the people who actually fund and resource these places. And so what you guys have shown is that you can actually have a university that's committed to excellence—an academic institution, I should say, that's committed to excellence. Where students from all walks of life, as you told me backstage, from very young students all the way up to PhD students can come together, discuss ideas, debate ideas, and have a free exchange and open debate amongst yourselves but also be fundamentally committed to the values that actually make it possible for all of us to live in these great societies together. So much of the academy has tried to destroy the foundations of Western civilization. You can actually have an institution that tries to build up the foundations of Western civilization, that corrects what needs to be corrected, that changes what needs to be changed but is fundamentally committed to the project. We want to have open debate, we want to have freedom of religion, we want people to walk down the street in public safety, we want borders, and we want the type of economies that provide good lives and good wages to our people. Foundationally, it's kind of crazy that so many Western academic institutions are not committed to any of these values and yet expect public support. You guys stand in defiance to that, so I thought it'd be fun to come and spend a morning with you. I could talk for hours about MCC but let's jump into political topics. You have now an issue in the United States where the Ukrainian influence services attempted an influence on the American elections. Can you describe a bit to the Hungarian audience what this is about? And why I ask it, it's because it's a topic as well in Hungary. We have this issue with the Druzhba pipeline, which is delivering oil through Ukraine to Hungary. Also, President Zelenskyy said that if Viktor Orbán doesn't change his behavior, he will send his soldiers to his private address. So also we have an issue with this question of Ukrainian influence. What is the situation in your country? And how do you see this in Hungary? So first of all, I wasn't even aware that Zelenskyy had said that he was going to send private soldiers to the Prime Minister's residence until yesterday. Viktor actually told me that and then I went and looked it up. I almost couldn't believe it's true, but it's true. That's completely scandalous. You should never have a foreign head of government or a foreign head of state threatening the head of government of an allied nation. It's preposterous, it's unacceptable. And what I find so interesting about—you know, there's a lot of conversation about foreign influence in our election. So you saw this back in 2016 where a lot of the American media said that it was a true scandal that the Russian government bought like $500,000 of Facebook advertisements during the 2016 campaign. And for what it's worth, I don't want Russia involved in our elections in the same way that I'm sure you don't want any other country involved in your elections. I've also been told that the Vice President of the United States coming and saying that Viktor Orbán is doing a good job and is a helpful statesman to the cause of peace—that's foreign influence. But what's not foreign influence is when the European Union threatens billions of dollars withheld from Hungary because you guys protect your borders. That's apparently not foreign influence. What's not foreign influence is when the Ukrainians shut down pipelines causing suffering among the Hungarian people in an effort to influence an election. That's allegedly not foreign influence. It doesn't pass the smell test. Foreign influence is when other governments threaten, cajole, and try to use economic influence to tell you how to vote. And that to the Hungarian people, whether you like Viktor Orbán or not, whether you agree with this or that policy, that is fundamentally an assault on your sovereignty. You know what I have never done? Even though we have of course a lot of economic power in the United States of America—we are the economic and military superpower of the world—we have never threatened Hungary saying, "If you don't vote for Viktor Orbán, you're not going to get this and this." We would never do that because we respect the Hungarian people enough to respect their sovereignty. The fact that so many foreign actors, whether they're transnational organizations like the bureaucrats in Brussels or whether it's foreign governments, are literally threatening the Hungarian people to "vote this way or we're going to exact our revenge on you"—that should make you very angry. You should vote against that for the simple reason that you are sovereign in this country. One of the coolest things about my visit is, late yesterday when it was closed down, we went and saw the crown of Saint Stephen in the Parliament. And I love the symbolism of this of course because it's one of the oldest, maybe the oldest surviving crown that exists in Western civilization. But it's there at the center of the parliamentary building. What does that mean? That means that sovereignty rests with the Hungarian people. The people are sovereign in Hungary. But for that to be true, the Hungarian people have to reject these foreign influence operations, vote for who they want to be their leader, and reject the Brussels bureaucrats. You were talking about the influence from Brussels. It's also interesting how, for example, free speech is in danger if you look at the tech companies and the influence on them from the Brussels bureaucrats. It's an important topic, it's an important question. But in connection to you, one of the largest Hungarian opposition leftist mainstream press wrote that there is no Ukrainian or Brussels influence in Hungary, only Russian influence. Do you know this from somewhere? I had not seen that observation, but again, I find it darkly ironic that people are accusing me of engaging in some kind of foreign influence. All that we're saying is that Viktor Orbán does a good job, because he does. He's a very, very important partner for peace with the American government, because he is. I hate to say this because they're obviously much bigger economies and much more populous countries in Europe, but most of the European political capitals have not been nearly as helpful to the cause of peace between Russia and Ukraine as Viktor Orbán has. And it's not because, by the way... I've sat in private meetings with Viktor where he has very, very capably summed up the Ukrainian position, where he said, "This is what the Ukrainians care about, this is what the Russians care about," because if you want to make peace, you have to understand what the two parties to the conflict actually think. It's not a betrayal to try to understand where both the Russians and Ukrainians are coming from; it's a necessary component to achieving a peaceful settlement. That's not Russian influence; that's a desire for peace. This is not American influence. Do you know how much—again, I would never do this—but you know how easy it would be for the United States to threaten Hungary economically in the same way that the EU has? We would never do that because we respect our friends enough to respect their democratic will. What's going on in the EU, what's going on in Brussels, what's going on with some of these foreign influence operations... I'm telling you, it is a scandal. It's the reason why I'm here. This is unprecedented. It's unprecedented for an American Vice President to come the week before an election. The reason why we're doing it is because we thought there was so much garbage happening against Viktor in this election that we had to show that there are actually a lot of people and a lot of friends across the world who recognize that Viktor and his government are doing a good job and they're important partners for peace. That's why we're here, but ultimately the Hungarian people are going to be sovereign because that's how it should be. We often hear that we are living in dangerous times. There is an ongoing situation in Iran now, but of course what is next to our border is the Russian-Ukrainian conflict. I think when you and President Trump won the elections, there was a window of opportunity to stop this. You would try to stop this. Then it didn't happen. I think the Brussels bureaucrats and the European elite said that and somehow made a pressure not to stop and not to make peace as soon as possible. What do you think were the motivations of the Brussels elite not to make this peace? And how long can it take to make it? Well, first of all, yeah, you know, the President has said this in the context of the Iran conflict, and he's said this in the context of some of the negotiations between Russia and Ukraine. As much as we love Europe, and as much as we love European peoples and cultures—I mean the United States, as you know, is very much a daughter nation of the European continent. We're very mindful of that fact. Of course we love the people, the culture, the everything about this place. But we do have some disagreements with the political leadership. In particular, why have the Europeans, while saying, by the way, that Russia is the biggest national security threat, made themselves completely dependent on unreliable sources of energy? If you think that Russia, an energy superpower, is the biggest threat, you should do everything in your power to make yourselves economically and energy dominant like President Trump has done in the United States. Our European friends have done the exact opposite. If you look at the cost of electricity in the United Kingdom, which is way underinvested in their energy resources, UK families are paying four times, five times, six times what United States citizens are paying for electricity. Now obviously I care most about America's citizens—that's who I'm elected to represent. But isn't it a scandal that middle-class Brits, that people who are working hard and playing by the rules, can't afford to heat their home, can't afford to transport themselves to work because their leadership has made energy so expensive? Isn't it a scandal that when normal people on the European continent try to talk about what's going on in their governments, they try to criticize their governments? When they try to exercise their rights of free speech, those governments then go to American social media companies and say, "you must censor the citizens of your own country because we don't want to hear them. We don't want to hear their free expression. We don't want to hear their free speech." There's just so much wrong with the political leadership, and I would particularly say the bureaucratic leadership in Brussels, right? People who aren't elected, who are not responsive to the democratic will and I think have made the European continent less secure, less free and less prosperous. The reason why we make criticism of European political capitals is because we want Europe to thrive and we think they're making a lot of mistakes. You know I, I've had a lot of conversations with European friends, with European heads of state and government, and it's always funny; they always say, "Well, you know, the United States just defies economic logic. You guys just have way higher economic growth, you have way lower energy costs." They say this as if it was something that happened through accident rather than through the policy choices of the President of the United States. It is not magical that the United States has lower energy costs than the United Kingdom; it's because we've made smart decisions and their leadership has not. They could change course and we hope that they will. I have the opportunity now to ask you about Iran. There are latest developments—I think very positive developments during the night that we received—that there is a ceasefire. What can we know more about the latest developments in the Middle East? Yes, that's why I was a little late this morning. I was up very late last night talking about that. Look, I do think there are good developments here. And let me just say a few things. First of all, what the President set out to do was decimate the Iranian military, decimate their ability to wage conventional war. And that military objective, as the President said yesterday and as I said yesterday, has been achieved. And because of that, what the President did is he basically issued an ultimatum to the Iranians. He said, "Open up the straits, stop trying to hold the world's economy hostage, and we'll engage in a ceasefire." And that's exactly the agreement that we came to last night. The Iranians have agreed to open up the straits, the United States has agreed to stop attacking—and not just the United States but also our allies have agreed to stop attacking—and that is the basis of this fragile truce that we have now, which is now, you know, 8 to 12 hours old. Now here's what I'll say about this. I've learned a lot about the Iranian system and a lot about the way the Iranians negotiate. As the President told me last night, the Iranians are better negotiators than they are fighters. I'm sure they don't like to hear that, but it's true. But here are a couple of things. So one, just in the response that we've seen from various segments of Iran, you have on the one hand people within Iran who have responded very favorably—the Foreign Minister who said, "Look, we agreed to the United States's terms, we'll do a ceasefire, we'll do a negotiation, we'll open the straits for moves and then we'll see if we can come to more agreement down the road." So some of the people have responded favorably and have said the right things. And then you have some people on social media within their system who are basically lying about what we've accomplished militarily. They're lying about the nature of the agreement, they're lying about the nature of the ceasefire. And so you have just even within their system—and this is why I say this is a fragile truce that we've already struck—you have people who clearly want to come to the negotiating table and work with us to find a good deal. And then you have people who are lying about even the fragile truce that we've already struck, and that's just an interesting thing about their system. The final point that I'll say about this is, the President of the United States has told me and he's told the entire negotiating team—the Secretary of State, the special envoy Steve Witkoff—he said, "Go and work in good faith to come to an agreement." That is what he has told us to do. If the Iranians are willing in good faith to work with us, I think we can make an agreement. If they're going to lie, if they're going to cheat, if they're going to try to prevent even the fragile truce that we've set up from taking place, then they're not going to be happy. Because what the President has also shown is that we still have clear military, diplomatic, and maybe most importantly, we have extraordinary economic leverage. So the President has told us not to use those tools; he's told us to come to the negotiating table. But if the Iranians don't do the exact same thing, they're going to find out that the President of the United States is not one to mess around. He's impatient to make progress, he has told us to negotiate in good faith, and I think if they negotiate in good faith, we will be able to find a deal, but that's a big if and ultimately it's up to the Iranians how they negotiate. I hope they make the right decision. We are here with a lot of talented Hungarian students. You talk a lot about education. How do you see the future of education, in which direction should it be developed? And do you know a bit about the Hungarian education system, especially the tertiary education system with our university system? What is your opinion about this? And how will you develop your education system? Well, I like that the Hungarian university system has imposed what I would call some democratic constraints here. The taxpayer should not be expected to fund radical gender ideology. The taxpayer should not be expected to fund sets of ideas—now people have free speech of course, they can say whatever they want to, but you're not entitled to taxpayer funding if you're advocating civil unrest and violence in the streets and gender ideology. Ultimately the taxpayers, they're the ones who decide what they're willing to fund and they should be the ones making that decision. So I really admire that Hungary has actually brought some common sense back to the connection between funding and the university system. You can say whatever you want, but in Hungary you don't get an unlimited subsidy if you want to say something that's really crazy or that promotes violence etc. I think that's a very important thing. I think in the American system we're making progress in that direction. We've said to the university system in the United States that you're no longer allowed to discriminate based on race. This sounds crazy, but three years ago, not only were they allowed to, but they were actively encouraged to discriminate against white students, against students of certain faiths. They were told—against Asian students too—they were actually told that you had to have a higher score if your skin color was a certain tone and a lower score if your skin color was a different tone. That's a violation of the American law, that's a violation of the American Constitution, that's a violation of basic morality, and the United States has now stopped that practice thanks to the leadership of the President and our Secretary of Education. So we're making progress I think in frankly reining the university system in: less discrimination, more promotion of free speech, and more recognition that taxpayer funding is not unlimited. And that's a very good thing. And I think, you know, fundamentally the education system has two roles. It has to prepare people to become effective and capable workers; they have to have the skills necessary to contribute to the modern economy. And they have to have the thought processes, they have to have the ability to think critically so that they can participate in a modern democracy. I think that's what the education system has to do. For too long in the West, what it did is it didn't teach any useful skills and it also brainwashed students, so it failed on both of those accounts. What we're trying to do in the United States, and I think you guys are trying to do in Hungary, is make sure that if you get a university degree, you have one of two things and hopefully both. You either have a useful skill for the workforce or you have the ability to think critically about society's challenges. And again, ideally you have both of those things; if you don't have those things, you shouldn't be paying for an education. Your book is also translated to Hungarian, your Netflix movie about you is also watched in Hungary. You are coming from a more poorer family, you are coming from a Rust Belt society of the United States and you attended an Ivy League university; you have a degree from Yale. What was the most important thing that you received during your education? That's a good question. You know I, I guess the most important thing that I got during my education at least in law school is I got a wife, because we met in law school and that's very important. I'd get in trouble if I didn't say that. But she's actually now pregnant with our fourth child, so obviously family is always the most important thing. Thank you. Thank you. Family is the most important and I do think that—this maybe is going to sound trite—but not just marriage and relationships romantically but also friendships. Some of the most important things that I got out of my educational experience, and I had a lot of good mentors who said people don't invest enough in their friendships. This is a great opportunity to build the kind of friendships, if you're a young person in university, to build the kind of relationships that really can last a lifetime. I think that's a very profound and powerful thing. You shouldn't... yes you want to get an education, yes you want to get a good job, but you also are spending four years with people, maybe longer and I think you should take that opportunity to really get to know what you're interested in, who you are and who you want to build a life with because that's one of the best things that you can get out of an education. But I think that just, if you set aside the relationships, the most important thing that I acquired—if I'm being honest, at Yale Law School in particular—is that I learned how elite social networks actually work. Okay? When I looked at this from the perspective of my hometown and I said, "Why are all the people who have the fancy jobs, why do they have this credential?" Or "Why do all the people who are on the Supreme Court or why all the people who have been in positions of political leadership, why do they all seem to know the same people and come from the same world?" And the answer is that as I think a lot of us know, whether it's in Brussels or Washington D.C. or New York City, these places become very insular. And there are things about that that just are—they're not good, they're not bad, it's just these places are insular. They're built around relationships, they're built around who you know. Many of the most important jobs in American society—I don't like this by the way, but it's just the truth—are based on who you know. And so it's important to sort of develop those relationships and it's important for conservatives in particular. I don't like this idea that conservatives shouldn't seek higher education. I don't think that we want to force everybody to go to college. But I think that if you're a conservative in the United States of America and you're interested in running for office or you're interested in being a judge, you do have to get higher education, okay? So we can accept that college isn't for everybody while also acknowledging that for certain jobs and certain professions college is necessary. But I think the very negative thing that I learned about it is that these social networks because they're so insular, they also become very self-reinforcing. I think there are people who have political power in the United States of America and I'm sure in Brussels too who have no idea what the majority of Americans actually think about a given issue. They've actually never talked to a normal American, an American who comes from outside of these social circles. I mean I would meet people who went to a fancy prep school who went to a fancy university who went to a fancy law school and had never spoken to a person from my home state of Ohio even though, you know, it's at certain parts it's a four or five-hour drive from Washington D.C. And so I think one of the fundamental things that has to change—I don't know about the Hungarian system, but about the American system—is that unless we make education more of a meritocracy, where people can come from all over the country based on merit rather than on who you know, I think the system is fundamentally doomed. And maybe the most important thing about eliminating racial discrimination, particularly against middle-class white kids in the American education context, is not just the moral part of it. Okay? The moral part of it I care a lot about. I don't want to discriminate against black kids, white kids, Latino kids; I don't want to discriminate against anybody based on your skin color. I think it's gross and immoral. But a very, very important part of it is that you're going to have actually greater geographic diversity in our universities because you're going to have people coming from all over the country to study. I think that will make those social networks less insular and less self-destructive. What would be your final message to the MCC students? Here are young individuals, 18, 19, 20. What would be the most important that they should be very careful and if you would be as young as they are now, how would you do your career? I'd say enjoy your youth while it lasts because one day you'll wake up and you'll be 41 and you'll have a glass of wine at dinner and wake up with a headache the next morning and be talking to this fine gentleman. So enjoy it while it lasts. But I think the piece of advice I'd give to you—and I give it to American students, American conservatives in particular—is resist the temptation to think that victory is immediate or that we're going to win back our civilization through instant gratification. I mean this institution was founded in 1996. It's far more influential than it was back then. But I think a lot of people—this is particularly true in the United States of America—if they see something that the administration does they don't like, they say, "that's not what we voted for, we're going to check out of politics." No, no, no. That's the exact wrong response. If we do something you don't like, the response should be to get more involved, to make your voice heard and to try to push things in the direction that you want them to be pushed. Our civilization was not built overnight, it's not going to be saved overnight. And so what I'd encourage you to do is stay involved, be patient, and don't let disappointment turn to checking out of the system entirely. There's way too much of that I would say in the Anglo system in the American system; there's way too much, you know, "I didn't like this thing that the Vice President said" or "I didn't like this thing that the President did" and "I'm going to completely check out." We call that blackpilling in the United States of America, and blackpilling is how you give power to the forces that are trying to destroy what our ancestors built. We need to take power back from those people and build the kind of institutions that can actually save our society. It will not happen overnight. It will not happen in the term of one Prime Minister or one President, but it will happen so long as we keep our faith in God and we work hard to achieve it. That's what we have to do. What 100 Years of American Finance Tells Us About Today

Invest Like The Best(47.1K) ·66:35 ·8.0K views · 2026-04-08
Patrick O'Shaughnessy (Host, Invest Like The Best) Alan Waxman (CEO, Sixth Street Partners)
The American financial system has transitioned into a "Factory Model" where the industrialization of fundraising and fee-related earnings (FRE) has led to deteriorating underwriting standards and dangerous asset-liability mismatches, particularly within the retail wealth channel.
TL;DR The American financial system has transitioned into a "Factory Model" where the industrialization of fundraising and fee-related earnings (FRE) has led to deteriorating underwriting standards and dangerous asset-liability mismatches, particularly within the retail wealth...

TL;DR

The American financial system has transitioned into a "Factory Model" where the industrialization of fundraising and fee-related earnings (FRE) has led to deteriorating underwriting standards and dangerous asset-liability mismatches, particularly within the retail wealth channel.

The Evolution of the U.S. Financial System

Alan Waxman categorizes the last century of American finance into three distinct systems:

  • System One (1933–1999): Defined by the Glass-Steagall Act and the separation of commercial banking (deposits) from investment banking (risk-taking).
      • Outcome: Provided 50 years of stability but was not optimized for global competition or maximum economic growth.
  • System Two (1999–2008): Triggered by the repeal of Glass-Steagall and deregulation.
      • Outcome: Led to massive leverage (20x–30x) and asset-liability mismatches as investment banks competed with integrated European powerhouses, ultimately culminating in the Global Financial Crisis (GFC).
  • System Three (2010–Present): Born from Basel III and Dodd-Frank, restricting bank leverage and shifting risk-taking to private capital.
      • The "Goldilocks" Ideal: Commercial banks serve as safe, government-backstopped pillars, while private capital (pension funds, endowments) provides risk capital with matched assets and liabilities.
      • Current Risk: This system is under pressure due to behavioral changes that began in 2018.

The "Factory Model" of Investing

Waxman warns against the industrialization of investment firms, which he calls the "Factory Model." This model prioritizes growth in assets under management (AUM) over artisanal investment returns.

  • Liability-Driven Strategy: The process starts with the industrialization of fundraising. Once a firm raises massive amounts of capital, it is forced to industrialize the asset side to deploy that capital quickly.
  • The Incentive (FRE Multiples): Markets began valuing Fee-Related Earnings (FRE) at 25x–30x multiples. This incentivizes CEOs to raise as much capital as possible to boost the firm's equity value, often at the expense of "carry" (performance-based earnings).
  • Deteriorating Underwriting: To maintain high deployment speeds, firms lower their standards, accepting "capped upside" (e.g., 10% returns) while allowing borrowers to strip away collateral or increase leverage.
  • Narrow Apertures: The model favors "simple" or "narrow" strategies (e.g., *only* direct lending) because they are easier to sell to the wealth channel, even if the market opportunity in that specific niche has soured.

The Wealth Channel and Liquidity Myths

The surge of private capital into the retail wealth channel has introduced systemic vulnerabilities similar to those seen in previous financial crises.

  • The "Semi-Liquid" Fallacy: Waxman asserts there is no such thing as "semi-liquid." You either have liquid assets or illiquid assets. Giving retail investors quarterly redemption rights on illiquid private loans creates a fundamental asset-liability mismatch.
  • Pro-cyclical Behavior: Retail and "mass affluent" investors are notoriously pro-cyclical—they provide capital when things are good and demand it back the moment volatility hits.
  • Stuck Assets: Many assets purchased in 2021 and early 2022 are now "stuck." If redemptions exceed limits (typically 5%), it creates the "noise" and stock price volatility currently seen in public private-equity firms.

Personal Productivity: The "Brain" Sheet

Waxman shares his personal system for maximizing Return on Time (ROT) and maintaining "Clarity of Purpose."

  • The One-Page System: Waxman organizes his life on a single sheet of paper divided into "Left Brain" (tactical) and "Right Brain" (creative) sections.
      • Left Brain: Contains his top five strategic priorities, high-priority people to call, and health goals (e.g., vitamin D, hip mobility). He rewrites this by hand weekly to "connect dots."
      • Right Brain: A 25-year archive of creative ideas, themes, and business-building thoughts.
  • Personal Business Plans: Every employee at Sixth Street is required to write a personal business plan. Waxman argues that people spend weeks evaluating companies but zero time evaluating their own "business plan" for the year.
  • Career Stages:
      • 20s: Pure education and asking "dumb questions."
      • 30s: Ambition and proving oneself, though still making "rookie" mistakes.
      • 40s: "Prime Time"—the decade where experience, mistakes, and energy align.
      • 50s: Transitioning to mentorship and teaching.

Creative Destruction and "Facing the Tiger"

  • AI as a Universal Disrupter: Waxman views AI as a tool for every industry, not just software. Companies that fail to adopt "agentic capabilities" to drive margins will be left behind.
  • Facing the Tiger: Sixth Street’s core ethos. It means running *toward* problems rather than away from them. When a mistake happens or a market shifts, the firm’s culture is to look at the issue head-on without pointing fingers.
  • The Multi-Strategy Advantage: Waxman argues that in a world of rapid change, having a "wide aperture" is essential. Narrow investment strategies are "crazy" because they cannot adapt when supply-demand dynamics shift.
"There is no such thing as semi-liquid. Anyone that’s an investor that’s been through a bunch of cycles—there’s liquid and then there’s illiquid." — Alan Waxman
"The Factory Model... starts on the liability side where you raise all the money, then you have it, then the behavioral change starts. The industrialization of the asset side follows." — Alan Waxman
"We look at the problems head-on, we look at them together and we don't run from them, we run to them. We run right at them. And that’s what 'face the tiger' is." — Alan Waxman

It is sort of the opposite of the old school artisanal investment model that's entirely focused on earning outstanding investment returns. First of all, I’m not an economic historian, okay? So what I’m going to do is tell the story of history as it relates to the current moment. I think you got to go back to like pre-1929 crash. And when you think about the American financial system as basically like the Wild Wild West, it was pretty unregulated. And there were many causes of the 1929 crash. There was poor monetary policy, agricultural recession, margin lending. But one of the main parts that caused it is you had this idea of commercial banks. So think about commercial banks: individuals go put their money into a bank as deposits. Commercial banks basically were in the same house as principal risk-taking activity, so the investment bank. So these were all part of the same thing. And as you can imagine, when that happens, there’s a massive conflict of interest. So really the story starts for the current moment, starts in 1933. This is after the 1929 crash. This is after 9,000 banks failed. Think about that. 9,000 banks failed. 1933 Glass-Steagall, probably one of the most important regulations that took place, and also the establishment of the FDIC which basically insured deposits for individuals at banks up to a certain limit. And Glass-Steagall basically said these commercial banks, which was deposit-taking institutions from individuals that just got really burned in the 1929 crash, basically become separated from the investment banks or at the time, think about principal risk-taking. So think about in today’s parlance, private capital investment banks. Those got separated. And that’s kind of the first system. When I think about the first system that explains kind of where we got to the current moment, let's just call it System One, 1933 to 1999. And when you look at post-World War II with this separation of commercial banks and investment banks, you basically have, really after World War II, kind of 50 years of pretty stable system. Other than the S&L crisis in the 1980s which was a big event, it was a pretty good system. But the system wasn’t optimized for economic growth because you only had really a pretty conservative commercial bank with a lot of guardrails providing finance across. So it’s low risk appetite. And again because one, the fixed income market hadn’t developed, which is part of the story here, but also because investment banks were more in the moving business than the storage business. Meaning they were like pricing securities to basically sell to other people. They weren’t pricing it to hold for their own balance sheet. Now that started to change as we get into the 80s but again broadly speaking for this first system from 1933 to 1999, it was working, it just wasn’t optimized. And your takeaway from this, the lesson from this is like with really good guardrails you can get long stability. You can get long stability, but again you also have to think about job creation and economic growth. And I think if there’s one criticism of the system—which by the way started, which is why the Glass-Steagall Act got repealed in 1999, I can talk about why it got repealed, sort of the steps leading up to that—is that it wasn’t optimized. And as you go to a more globalized world and you’re competing with say European banks, you become less and less competitive. So in a non-globalized world, it was probably okay, but as we got to a more globalized world, it wasn’t really optimized to maximize economic growth for the country. Okay so we get to the mid-late 90s. What happens in addition to new competitive pressures? Just walk us through the transition into what becomes System Two. So at the time, again we’ve got separation of investment banks and commercial banks. All of a sudden European banks started to, who weren’t part of the same Glass-Steagall regulation, they started to unite with each other. So commercial banks and investment banks in Europe started to come together, which started to put the American commercial banks at a big disadvantage. And not only were they coming together but they were also taking on more leverage than what was allowed with the guardrails of American commercial banks. So as a result of that, as you can imagine, all the commercial banks and many market participants are saying, "Hey we can’t really compete against some of these European guys." 1998 Deutsche Bank bought Bankers Trust and that was kind of like a moment. You had Citibank announced that it was merging with Travelers which at the time when they announced the merger, it would actually wasn’t allowed under Glass-Steagall under the current regulation. So that’s what sort of led up to it. I think it’s a couple things. Globalization, like now all of a sudden you’re competing against Europeans who have—think about it—they can provide services and balance sheet and capital. You’re at a pretty big disadvantage. So System One started to kind of get less competitive as we moved into a globalized world. And that sort of led to 1999 when Glass-Steagall was repealed. And so what comes in its place? It’s basically just deregulation. It’s deregulation and literally after that you saw literally a wave of mergers of combining commercial banks and investment banks. So you saw JP Morgan Chase, there’s many others, but there’s always with everything there’s knock-on effects. That came together, created these powerhouses that could compete with what was going on in Europe. But now you had all these investment banks that weren't commercial banks, so think about my old firm Goldman Sachs and many others. Now they had to start competing where they weren’t, they didn’t have access to cheap capital because they weren’t a commercial bank. They had to compete with combined investment banks and commercial banks because a lot of the commercial banks, both in Europe and the US, they started to use their balance sheet to get investment banking business. So what did all the investment banks do? They started to leverage up. And that’s one of the other stories leading into the system is sort of the development of the fixed income market. So think about corporate bonds, mortgage-backed securities, asset-backed securities, sovereign debt. That went literally from the 80s to the 90s, went from like 7 trillion dollars to 14 trillion dollars. So these are all financing mechanisms that could finance the investment banks to basically allow them to leverage up. And that’s what started to happen. So literally from the time of Glass-Steagall being repealed, you had commercial banks uniting with investment banks both in US and Europe. You had leverage going up, like leverage went up for commercial banks in some cases 20, 30 times leverage. And all the investment banks were operating with leverage because they had to take on leverage to be able to compete with the combined commercial banks and investment banks. And then nine years later what happened? You had the GFC. Now just to be clear, there’s a lot of—it’s a polarizing debate of how much attribution the repeal of Glass-Steagall had on the GFC. I think there was definitely some attribution to it. I don’t think that was clearly not the only reason. Look, my view it’s some combination, but ultimately it had to do with the system and the set of incentives. And in that case, after putting all this together, it was a lack of guardrails that existed in sort of the first system we spoke about. And in System Two is the reminder I guess, or the lesson that it’s the combination of liquidity or asset-liability mismatches and leverage that basically is the cocktail for every historical financial crisis. Like one of those two or both are involved. Leverage always plays a role, but they’re all connected, but just the mismatching of assets and liabilities. You could be the best investor in the world making the best illiquid investments, but if someone comes and asks for your money in a quarter when you haven’t had time to actually have that investment play out the way that you underwrote it to do, you’re going to be a bad investor. You’re going to get caught out of your option and you might have to sell at a deep discount. So I think there's a few things. I think it’s one, anytime you bring retail or individuals, so think about people depositing into a bank, next to principal risk-taking activity. I think that’s one thing. I think the second thing is just anytime you mismatch assets and liabilities. And then the third thing again going back to what we talked about earlier is like what are the incentives, what are the guardrails, and what’s the market structure. Okay so then what happens? So obviously we know about global financial crisis is terrifying and the reaction is many things, but what is installed post-GFC that sets the seed for I guess what we'll call the current system, System Three. So in 2010, two things happened. First is Basel III was passed by G20 nations. I’ll explain what that is. And the second thing is Dodd-Frank. When you think about Basel III, so this applies across all commercial banks and by the way a number of investment banks that were not commercial banks were forced to become commercial banks as a result of this. Those commercial banks, and this is really a Basel III thing, had restrictions on capital which for your audience think about that as leverage, so the amount that they could be leveraged up so they didn’t get leveraged up 30 to 1 or 40 to 1 like they did pre-GFC. And the second thing is restrictions on liquidity and liquidity is basically through a bunch of shock scenarios, a bunch of things going wrong, do you have enough liquidity to meet all your obligations. I think that was a key part of it. Dodd-Frank was more aimed at, and the Volcker rule, that didn’t really last that long, was really aimed at the principal investing activity. I’d say it’s more for the commercial banks, it was more Basel III but Dodd-Frank played a big role certainly in the short term. So how would you explain just System Three and its guardrails and incentives to people out there. So System Three in my opinion, and we took like 125 years to get here, has the potential to be the best system American finance has ever had. Because when you think about commercial banks are deposit-taking institutions, by the way that are basically backstopped by the government, insured by the government through the FDIC. So think about GFC, there was a bailout, the taxpayer bailout. That’s not good for society, that’s not good for the middle class. That was not a good outcome for America. For those institutions, having restrictions on capital or leverage and liquidity where they’re doing sort of lower risk-taking activity to finance the system, that’s a good pillar of any financial system. Conversely on the other side and this is where the current moment starts to come in, is now you’ve got private capital coming in. So think about principal risk-taking activities, private capital was filling in the gap. And with the exception of hedge funds and really REITs, those were matched assets and liabilities. So you think about private equity, private real estate, private infrastructure, private credit, they never had someone that could literally ask for their money back. Or they didn’t have depositors saying they need to get their money back. They couldn’t get it back because of illiquid assets. So that just to put in context, private capital from pre-GFC to post-GFC was about 2 trillion pre-GFC, it’s grown to around 14-15 trillion. Private credit, which is in the news today, grew from 500 billion to about 2 trillion what it is today. So massive growth and this sort of filled the gap for that principal risk-taking capital, provide risk capital to all parts of the American economy, which is a good thing. And I would say up until 2018, the system was working great. You had commercial banks, deposit-taking institutions, effectively backstopped by the government doing safer things. And then you had matched assets and liabilities where an investor, a set of assets couldn’t get caught out of their option, providing the risk capital. That’s a pretty good system. And until we started to see behavioral changes in 2018. Just to like put a pin on an elegant, well-designed system of guardrails and incentives. The commercial kind of model where it’s lower risk and protected or backstopped, and higher risk-seeking capital where the assets and liabilities are matched, is a good system. It’s a good system. All crises are generally caused from not credit issues or other issues, they might start in other issues, but it’s mismatching assets and liabilities. So you mentioned this year 2018 as being a pivotal point. I want to explain that transition but it feels important, you and I have talked about this notion of yours of the factory model before. We’re going to go into that in more detail but just to plant the seed in people’s minds, define the factory model just briefly and then I want to talk about how we, what happened to get us kind of transitioned and the incentives towards that model. Sure. So the way that we define the factory model in our industry is there’s two parts to it and then there’s an output. The two parts to it are, first part is the industrialization of the fundraising process, where I’d say liability gathering. So literally raising as much capital as you possibly can, literally as fast as you can. So that’s sort of the industrialization of the liability side of the fundraising side. That comes first. And then what comes second is then, as a result of that, the industrialization of the asset side. So think about investing. If you’re on an investment team and all of a sudden your firm has a lot of money to invest and it’s just sitting there and maybe there’s a time stamp on it, all of a sudden your behavior has to start to change. Because you have to deploy that money much quicker. And what’s the best way to raise a lot of capital quickly? Big as capital source is direct. It’s raise simple, so make it very simple, make it very narrow because if it’s wide, like that’s too hard to explain. So you want to make it as narrow as possible. And you’re also willing to take, let’s say, make concessions on the type of capital you raise. So meaning, maybe it’s got a term where they can ask for your money back. So it starts to, instead of perfectly matched assets and liabilities, maybe you’re willing to start to not have perfectly matched assets and liabilities because you want to raise it as fast as possible. And again when people hear this, they’re going to think I’m only talking about the bigger firms in our industry, but it filtered down to mid-sized firms, smaller firms for a whole bunch of reasons. But this whole factory model behavior started to reveal itself in 2018. And just to draw like a visual, the visual that’s coming to mind on the asset side is I think of like an artisan making like a horse saddle or something by hand and then I get an order for 100,000 horse saddles—like I can’t make it by hand, I gotta make a factory. That is exactly the way to think about because it’s a different model when you’re building that horse saddle versus you get a massive order. But one point’s important is that it starts always on the liability side and then it goes to the asset side, and then you get kind of the current moment that I know we’re going to talk about. And it starts on the liability side because why? Because if you just all of a sudden go to that example, it’s a really good example of the horse saddle and all of a sudden if you don’t have like a factory that can produce 100,000 on the artisanal side you’re not ever having to think about it. Like you could have that industrialization of the asset side but if you don’t have the liability, if you’re not liability constrained, you’re not going to change behavior because you don’t have the capital to go do that. You’ll run out of money in five days. So it’s got to start on the liability side where you raise all the money, then you have it, then the behavioral change starts. So these two things, it’s first liability side it starts the industrialization and as a result of that it goes to the asset side. Which is interesting because basically I don’t know if you added up every conversation I’ve ever had with an investor, 98% of the time spent is on the asset side. What are you investing in and why? Exactly. And by the way that’s okay if you have perfectly asset matched assets and liabilities, it’s okay. But let’s imagine a world where every investor you spoke about had a term in their agreement after three years the investor had the option to call their money back. That would probably be something you want to be talking about a lot. By the way prior to 2018, going back to the financial system, the private capital pool was pretty perfectly matched assets and liabilities. And obviously like if everything was frictionless and I was a GP, I would of course have matched liabilities. Like if I could just snap as much capital as I wanted into existence, yeah of course like I want to have no problems. So what was going on, what’s the series of events starting in 2018, what were the first examples of this and then how has it like evolved? The first signal is underwriting. Because investing or lending, you can invest as much money as you want, you can lend as much money, that’s not the skill. The skill is investing, it’s that artisanal behavior. So when you start to—and again it wasn’t just everyone talks about private credit—but we started to see it in every asset class. We started to see it in real estate, we started to see it in infrastructure, we started to see it in private credit. It wasn’t actually bad, but we started to see behaviors like terms that you would never do because obviously when you lower your underwriting standards, guess what happens? Your deployment pace can go up. You have an origination engine, you’re sourcing all these deals and let’s say you’re in artisanal, you might have a hit rate of you know half a percent you look at. If you lower your underwriting standards, your hit rate on deals that you might do might go to 2% or 3%. It’s literally all in your control. So I think we started to see it but it wasn’t full-fledged factory model industrialization. Covid happened and then post-Covid it was like game on for the factory model both on the liability raising side and also on the asset side. Literally that behavior started to accelerate in incredible ways right after Covid. And was that mostly, I know this has come from everywhere the capital, the liability has come from lots of different pockets, but my mind goes to like the wealth channel that everyone’s talking about now, institutional channel as well. But yeah maybe put a little more color on like where it actually came from, is coming from. So what started to change in 2018 is there are these things called SMAs, so separately managed accounts. So prior to 2018 for the most part the private capital ecosystem was basically funneled through funds. So think about co-mingled funds, lots of investors coming into one fund to pursue a certain strategy. And all of a sudden there started to be every conversation with every LP was basically: we want an SMA. So we want a fund of one just to do XYZ for us. You go to an LP, you basically say, "Hey we’re going to raise 500 million dollars or 100 million dollars and we’re going to do direct lending, or we’re going to do private equity, or we’re going to do real estate." And all of a sudden there started to be a proliferation where literally three years prior it was not in any conversation, every conversation was SMAs. And what it is, it was just the industry starting to raise capital from the institutional channel—so not wealth, the institutional channel—so pension funds, sovereign wealth funds, to some extent endowments, raise as much capital as possible in the simplest form. It started on the institutional side with SMAs, but the growth in institutional SMAs started to really taper off. So the next place where the industry started to go was the wealth space. And the wealth space in general just from a historical perspective, it is typically the easiest to raise, the simplest to raise. It’s typically the cheapest—that doesn’t mean that they’re not smart, just the cheapest—but the other characterization of the wealth space is that it’s always easiest to raise in the procyclical environments when things are going really well. But when things start to not go well, the wealth space or retail or individuals want their money back quickly. I just want to level set on that, it’s an important concept. And then that’s where it started to go. And that’s sort of got us to again, it got us to one of the symptoms that are in here today. But the one thing I want to point out and we’ll talk about the current moment, is that the SMA was a symptom. What’s going on in the wealth system, what the wealth system is, is a symptom. You know when you think about some of the stuff you see in stuck private assets where there’s so many assets around the world in private real estate, private infrastructure, private equity that literally were companies or assets that were bought in really post-Covid of 2021, early 22, paid way too much. Like they’re stuck assets. All that stuff is symptoms. The root cause of this is the change of behavior patterns of the factory model. That’s like the root cause and again one of the things that’s not frustrating but unfortunate is that everything that is covered in the media is just talking about the symptoms and not actually getting to the root cause. Yeah so it’s good to set back on what’s the System. So what are the incentives, what are the guardrails, what’s the market structure. The incentives in this story, the story of the factory model starts to correspond with FRE multiples. What is FRE? FRE stands for fee-related earnings. Fee-related earnings is basically your management fee profit. So you raise a fund, it’s got a management fee on it, you got a set of expenses, and what’s left over that is your fee-related earnings. These things for our industry started to—you know traded between in let’s say early 2010s, call it 10 to 15 times FRE. In 2018 when all this started, it stepped up to call it 15 to 20 times. Obviously depends on the comp set. And before this current moment, we’re at you know 25 to 30 times plus. That’s where it is. And by the way if you go back to the early passing of Basel III and Dodd-Frank, there was a massive secular opportunity to fill the gap that was left from commercial banks being constrained and then sort of it found—the system found—its sort of steady-state place. But in order to keep growing, and again it’s the whole industry, what do they do? Many participants adopted the factory model. And is maybe the crass way to say this, like in the factory model the GP, the founder of the firm, stands to make a lot more money from the equity of their GP than from like the carry they would earn through investing or something like this? What are the most common in your mind telltale signs of a firm that’s in this model? I do think there needs to be a major recalibration of behavior and the way that people approach this wealth channel because if you go back to what we talked about earlier, anytime society or finance system puts wealth or retail individuals next to principal risk-taking, if you look throughout history, that’s where problems start to happen. Most of it’s been with commercial banks because that’s been the primary pillar of the finance system, but now with this new pillar in private capital, it’s starting to sort of touch risk capital and it’s starting to become more asset-liability mismatch. Ultimately in every ecosystem whether it’s direct lending or private equity or real estate or infrastructure, they go through supply-demand dynamics. Sometimes there’s a supply of capital that is really high and demand is low, that’s probably not a good time to invest. And sometimes demand of capital is really high and supply capital is really low. That’s—again not certainly—but probably pretty good time to invest. I have an off the wall one for you, it’s been deeply impactful on me. Can you explain this like paper one sheet system for how you get everything done and like track what you do?
