Absolute complete, complete clarity on what those five things are, and I have a box for each of those five things. So that’s kind of like five boxes on each of those things. Then I have, like, high priorities because, again, those have different cadence to them. You know, everything has a different cadence, which is why I think you have to see everything together. The boxes on the page change every year, just like our themes every year change. Like, everything has to change every year because it goes back to adapting, because the world’s always changing so quickly. If you’re not adapting yourself, then you’re going to get lost in this world. So I’ll have my five strategic priorities of my time; I’ll have people—like really people I really want to focus on, those could be internal or external. I also have on there my health because, despite drinking this, I think about it because I actually think I have to be healthy to be able to do my job. What would be an example of something in the health that gets written down in health? I’ve got on there Vitamin D; like, I’m very focused on Vitamin D. I’ve had an old soccer injury, so I’m focused on left hip mobility, so this year’s a big year—it's like my hip mobility. I mean, this is... But it’s something you just see every day and it’s just constant. I see it every day, yeah, everything. It’s also the personal side so I keep balance. I think it is an intention system, but it's also—it's also a return on time system and an ability to dynamically prioritize. You talk to younger people, you know, who are just coming up through the business—even some older people still don't know how to prioritize their time. It’s really hard to do because, literally, you could spend all your time on one thing. So, how to, like, manage the time and just getting—being able to see that in, you know, in your brain or in the matrix—I think, you know, that's kind of how I think about it. Another thing that you really—last time we talked—really stuck in my head was, I just turned 40 and we were talking about the sort of opportunity you have from age 40 to 50, which got me wondering about 20 to 30 and 30 to 40. If you—if you think back on the major, like, eras of building and managing a life's work and a career and tied to specific ages, what have you learned? 20 to 30 for me was education, just almost like business school because I didn’t go to business school. Just learning just as much as I could, asking as many dumb questions as possible. Literally, your 20 to 30, you don’t—you think you know stuff, but if you haven’t been through cycles or made a lot of mistakes and seen other people make mistakes and see people make good decisions and what a good long-term decision or short-term decision is, you don’t really know anything from your 20 to 30. 30 to 40, you're kind of, you know, you're—you're incredibly ambitious. You're still learning, but you're, like, trying to prove yourself. You know, you're out there and you're—you know, we started Sixth Street with my partners when I was 33 or 34, so I didn’t know what I didn’t know. I mean, I knew a lot, but it’s like you’re going through that but you haven’t made enough mistakes yet to, like, really sort of refine everything, sort of your—your craft. And you get to 40 to 50, and 40 to 50, it’s like—it’s all together. You’ve made—if you’ve—if you’ve spent time, you know, learning and again, continue to learn, you’ve made enough mistakes. You kind of—you really know who you are at that point, know who you are as an investor and how you approach things. It’s like prime time. And then you get to 50 and then, you know, you’re—you’re trying to really focus on being a mentor, developing the next generation, and just trying to provide that voice in the room not only in terms of investing but also leadership, management, and really just trying to be a teacher to your team, but also a learner because I still learn a lot from them. But you know, 40 to 50, you’re in the—you’re—it’s go time. In go time, one of the questions that I’ve been asking everybody—because I’m just selfishly curious about it, and at this age it feels like the right time to ask—is around the measurement of success. And Kevin Kelly, the former—one of the founders of Wired Magazine—has this amazing idea which is, like, your success definition should be extremely bespoke to you. Like, traditional measures of success are—are traps: money, power, fame, etc. And I heard someone, a founder recently, say something like he measures success through, like, the degree of radical self-respect. Like, success means complete self-respect. And obviously that then means lots of other things. But I’m so curious: how, if I’m going into prime time or something, I don’t want to waste that. And so the objective function of prime time needs to be, like, success. That's good wisdom. Let’s hit the mistake that, you know, people fall into, is this whole idea of money, fame, fortune. That’s a cup—once you start to prioritize that, that’s a cup that will never get filled. You keep trying to fill the cup, and the cup keeps getting bigger and bigger. That cup never gets full. So I think that’s one of the problems I think people make in our industry is that they think the—the, the, the cup—and I even hear people say, "Oh, it’s easy for you to say where you are now"—this is something my dad taught me when I was, like, 10 years old. So this is not new. I can say this now; this is what it was. It was never the thing. For me, it’s like—I, I just want to do great things, be excellent, and do it with great people that share my values and do things the right way. And that’s on the business side. And I want to do all that in a way—and be excellent in—is not competing against anyone else, but competing against ourselves. But do so in a way where I’m the best dad, I’m the best husband. And you know, it’s getting one without the other—I just think you’re going to be 80 years old, you’re looking at your mirror, and what was the purpose of life? There’s no purpose. Like, the purpose of life for me—and again, it’s certainly not about the cup, that’s definitely never been it—it’s about all those relationships you form and those experiences you go through with people. Like, when you’re 80 or 85 years old, you’re looking back. Hopefully I’m healthy because I looked at my sheet a lot of times, and it’s those relationships and those experiences that I think drive to a fulfilled life. And obviously it starts with your family, but you know, I have a lot of Hawaiian friends. Your hui. Hui is like a term for, like, your—your group, your posse. It’s like having those experiences of climbing up the mountain together, and that’s to me what it’s all about. And if you are around the right people, you have the clarity of purpose, you have the right values, you have the right culture, and you’re going up the mountain together, it’s so fun. And you never have to, like, question first principles. How you’re going to do business, trying to do the right way—and it’s called, you know, what we call clean living. But again, doing that at the expense of not spending time with your family, I think that would be pretty unfulfilling to me. Last time, I got to ask you my traditional closing question, so I have to come up with a new one this time. One of my favorite things from our first discussion—we even, you sent us the visual, which I love—is the—is the concept of facing the tiger. Maybe you can remind us what that means. But literally, like, when you—I thought you were kidding in the conversation, but like, literally off the elevator is a giant tiger in your office, which is so funny. I like the principle a lot, but I’m also curious what it means to apply that principle for you and Sixth Street today in this fascinating, dynamic environment. Face the tiger—it’s one of the core ethos of—of Sixth Street, which is: there’s hard things in this world. We're going to make mistakes, we're going to have problems, but when those problems happen, instead of pointing fingers, it's like we have just a saying from day one of our firm: that we look at the problems head-on, we look at them together, and we don't run from them, we run to them. We run right at them. And that’s what face the tiger is. And I think for the environment we’re in—and this is what I literally told our entire firm—is that we’re in a world where the pace of change is rapidly accelerating. And if you think the pace of change is accelerated now, it—it’s going to just continue, and continue to accelerate. Which is why, by the way, from an investing standpoint—going back to what we said earlier—the idea that you’re going to have a narrow investment strategy when the world’s changing so much... it's crazy. You’re going to have oscillating supply-demand dynamics of good time, bad time; like, it’s just crazy to raise a too-narrow strategy unless you, like, put a governor on the amount of capital raising. But I think the biggest thing when you look at the human being is human beings in general don't like change. There are a small percentage that, like, thrive in chaos and love it and step up, like Michael Jordan. Like, he loved chaos; he'd go down and—it’s like his heart rate’s low and you know, hit—hit a game-winning shot. But most human beings don't like change. And as we start to go through this pace of change, there's obviously a lot of anxiety: is AI going to take my job, is it not? And our whole thing is like, "Look, we got to face the tiger. It’s going to change whether we like it or not; it's going to happen." Yeah, there’s the stuff from AI, but what are you going to do about it? And that’s what we say to people. It's like, "Look, we got to face the tiger and just remember you get one life. Do you want to be average or do you want to be excellent?" And that's kind of how I talk to our people. So, we just—you keep talking about it enough and they sort of get in the right headspace, so when change happens or there's disruption or something goes wrong, they've got sort of a tool that they can use—let's say, face the tiger—to be able to approach it. And we try to just get that in our firm. So—and again, I think I said this last time—when problems happen or something goes wrong, we're like most people: we're like, "Good, let's go. It's like game time, let's go." And that’s kind of just the way we've been since day one and, I think to some extent, the way we are as people. I wish I could do this with you every year. I hope we do. Thank you so much for your time. Thank you so much, Patrick. Appreciate it.

Josh Shapiro on Trump, Iran War Chaos, Israel's Failure, the Economy, and 2028 Race

All-In Podcast(1.1M) ·61:52 ·30.5K views · 2026-04-08
Josh Shapiro (Governor of Pennsylvania) Jason Calacanis (Co-host, All-In Podcast) Chamath Palihapitiya (Co-host, All-In Podcast)
> "The way we start the conversation is by recognizing permits are critically important to economic development...
TL;DR: Governor Josh Shapiro details his "Pennsylvania Model" of governance—prioritizing operational efficiency ("Getting Shit Done"), pro-growth economic policies, and aggressive fraud prosecution—as a centrist blueprint for the Democratic Party to counter federal "chaos and...

TL;DR: Governor Josh Shapiro details his "Pennsylvania Model" of governance—prioritizing operational efficiency ("Getting Shit Done"), pro-growth economic policies, and aggressive fraud prosecution—as a centrist blueprint for the Democratic Party to counter federal "chaos and corruption."

The "Pennsylvania Model": Operational Efficiency & Economic Growth

  • "GSD" (Get Shit Done) Philosophy: Shapiro emphasizes a results-oriented approach to government, focusing on making the bureaucracy move "at the speed of business."
  • Permitting Reform:
      • Money-Back Guarantee: Pennsylvania now offers a guarantee on permits; if the state misses a deadline, the fee is refunded. Out of 40 million permits issued, only five refunds have been required.
      • Speed Improvements: The state has moved from bottom-five to top-five nationally in permitting speed. Professional licenses (e.g., barbers) that previously took 20 days are now issued same-day.
  • Tax Policy: Shapiro has cut taxes seven times during his tenure to increase competitiveness. He explicitly opposes wealth taxes or the seizure of unrealized capital gains, arguing that a pro-growth environment is more effective for long-term revenue.
  • Workforce Development:
      • Removing Degree Barriers: Shapiro signed an executive order removing college degree requirements for 60% of state government jobs, focusing on skills and "Generation Tool-Belt" trades.
      • Trade Investment: Tripled funding for vocational-technical (Vo-Tech) and apprenticeship programs.

Accountability and Anti-Fraud Initiatives

  • Prosecuting Fraud: Drawing on his background as Attorney General, Shapiro maintains a zero-tolerance policy for the theft of taxpayer dollars.
      • Medicaid Fraud: Pennsylvania ranks at the top of the country for Medicaid fraud prosecutions and recoveries.
      • COVID Relief: Aggressive prosecution of PPP loan fraud to ensure funds reach intended recipients.
  • Public Safety:
      • Staffing: Hired 2,000 additional police officers.
      • Results: Reported a 12% drop in violent crime and a 42% reduction in fatal gun violence across the Commonwealth.

The Future of the Democratic Party & 2028

  • 2024 Election & VP Selection: Shapiro dismissed claims that he was passed over for the Vice Presidential nomination due to his faith, stating he withdrew from consideration 48 hours before the announcement to focus on governing Pennsylvania.
  • Democratic "Big Tent": While acknowledging a "Socialist" wing of the party, Shapiro advocates for a "big tent" where disagreement is healthy but common ground is the priority for governing.
  • Strategy for 2028: He argues the party must provide a "calm, competent" alternative to the "chaos, cruelty, and corruption" he attributes to Donald Trump.
  • Critique of Congress: Shapiro describes current Congressional leadership (specifically Speaker Johnson) as "pathetic" for acting as a "rubber stamp" for the executive branch rather than a constitutional check.

Geopolitics: Israel, Iran, and Antisemitism

  • Antisemitism vs. Policy: Shapiro insists on separating the condemnation of antisemitism from the critique of Middle East policy. He argues antisemitism must be "universally condemned" without nuance, while Middle East policy requires deep nuance.
  • Netanyahu Critique: He characterizes Prime Minister Netanyahu as a leader who has isolated Israel and "fractured" bipartisan American support through dangerous, extremist-aligned policies.
  • War with Iran: Shapiro labels the escalating conflict with Iran a "war of choice" by the Trump administration, criticizing the lack of a defined mission or exit strategy. He argues that American interests are poorly served when the U.S. is "led around" by other nations.

Notable Quotes

"The way we start the conversation is by recognizing permits are critically important to economic development... if I can give you as a CEO predictability to know your business is going to be opening in six months instead of three years, you’re going to want to invest here." — Gov. Josh Shapiro
"I think the Congress of the United States, the leadership there... they are frankly kind of sad, pathetic people. They have given away their power to folks who are corrupt and to folks who are not acting in an honorable way." — Gov. Josh Shapiro
"On the issue of antisemitism, we have got to be in a place where we universally condemn it... On the issue of Israel and the Middle East... there’s a lot of nuance there. You can be super critical of Israel and you could love your Jewish neighbors and friends." — Gov. Josh Shapiro

The Era of AI Agents | Aaron Levie on The a16z Show

a16z(263.0K) ·58:29 ·13.3K views · 2026-04-08
Aaron Levie (CEO, Box) Erik Torenberg (Host, The a16z Show) Steven Sinofsky (Host, The a16z Show)
- **Scale of Usage:** Aaron Levie (CEO, Box) posits a future with 100 to 1,000 times more agents than human users, necessitating a move toward software built primarily for agent interaction via APIs, CLIs, or Model Context Protocol (MCP).
TL;DR: As AI agents increasingly outnumber human users, enterprise software must pivot from GUI-first to API/CLI-first architectures, though this transition faces significant hurdles in security, "system of record" stability, and a massive underestimation of future compute...

TL;DR: As AI agents increasingly outnumber human users, enterprise software must pivot from GUI-first to API/CLI-first architectures, though this transition faces significant hurdles in security, "system of record" stability, and a massive underestimation of future compute economics.

The Shift to Agent-First Software Architecture

  • Scale of Usage: Aaron Levie (CEO, Box) posits a future with 100 to 1,000 times more agents than human users, necessitating a move toward software built primarily for agent interaction via APIs, CLIs, or Model Context Protocol (MCP).
  • Interface Evolution: While humans struggle with "algorithmic thinking" and creating flowcharts, agents excel at navigating complex software surfaces.
      • Computer Use vs. Code: Levie notes a shift from agents purely generating code to "computer use," where agents interact with SaaS tools like humans do, but at massive scale.
      • The "Mezzanine Step": Current AI is becoming a consumption layer, mapping natural language to existing software capabilities that humans previously found too complex to use (e.g., advanced Excel or SAP functions).
  • Abstraction Layers: Steven Sinofsky (Former Microsoft President) argues that "the job moves up a rung." Just as spreadsheets didn't eliminate bankers but changed their skill sets, agents will handle lower-level execution while humans move to higher-level system coordination.

Security, Liability, and the "System of Record"

  • Agent Identity vs. Human Employees: Levie argues agents cannot yet be treated like human employees because they lack privacy rights and are highly susceptible to prompt injection.
      • Liability: A human employee is accountable for their own mistakes; an agent’s actions are a direct liability for the user who deployed it.
      • Security Paradox: If an agent has access to sensitive data (like M&A documents), it can be social-engineered far more easily than a human, creating a massive risk for enterprises.
  • Integration on the Fly: There is a fundamental tension between the desire for "on-demand integration" and the need for stability.
      • CFO/CIO Pushback: Sinofsky notes that enterprise leaders fear "agents running wild" and breaking systems of record by creating unauthorized or poorly constructed integrations between legacy systems (e.g., SAP and Workday).
      • The Diffusion Gap: Startups will move faster because they have "nothing to blow up," while large enterprises like JP Morgan will remain in a "read-only" phase for years to manage risk.

The Economic Miscalculation of AI

  • Wall Street’s Linear Error: Sinofsky argues that Wall Street is off by an "order of magnitude" because they view the AI market as a fixed revenue pie.
      • Historical Precedent: Just as the PC and Cloud were initially viewed as finite replacements for existing MIPS or servers, AI will likely result in an asymptotic explosion of resource consumption and new business models.
  • The Engineering Token Budget: A looming conflict for engineering leaders is determining what percentage of R&D should go toward tokens vs. human salaries.
      • Usage-Based Pricing: The high COGS (cost of goods sold) of tokens is forcing the industry toward usage-based models, a shift as significant as the move from perpetual licenses to SaaS.
  • Token Rationing: Levie predicts a short-term "rationing" phase where companies must decide which employees or experiments get priority access to compute until data center capacity catches up.

The Future of the Enterprise IT Stack

  • Standardization vs. Meritocracy: Agents are "smart" at choosing tools based on performance and semantics rather than marketing. This may force software vendors to build better systems rather than just better sales stories.
  • System Fragmentation: Sinofsky warns that agents might inadvertently fragment IT systems by spinning up de facto "new systems of record" in middleware, leading to security vulnerabilities similar to the "shadow IT" era of the early web.
  • The "Transistor Moment": While current token costs are high, Sinofsky expects a "transistor moment"—an algorithmic or hardware breakthrough that will eventually make the current obsession with token budgeting irrelevant.

***

"Wall Street's economics are off by an order of magnitude on how big the opportunity is... they have this fixed revenue pie, but the amount of software being written is going asymptotic." — Steven Sinofsky

Beyond the IT Headlines | Inside the AI Data Factory: How Enterprise AI Gets Built

Everpure(26.5K) ·25:13 ·12 views · 2026-04-08
Kaycee Lai (VP of AI, Everpure) Shawn Rosemarin (Host, Beyond the IT Headlines)
The enterprise AI landscape has shifted from experimental training to "always-on" production (inference), requiring a fundamental transition toward "AI Data Factories"—architectural blueprints rather than single products—where data is continuously pr...
TL;DR The enterprise AI landscape has shifted from experimental training to "always-on" production (inference), requiring a fundamental transition toward "AI Data Factories"—architectural blueprints rather than single products—where data is continuously processed by autonomous...

TL;DR

The enterprise AI landscape has shifted from experimental training to "always-on" production (inference), requiring a fundamental transition toward "AI Data Factories"—architectural blueprints rather than single products—where data is continuously processed by autonomous agents to drive core business outcomes.

From AI Training to "Inference-First" Operations

  • Defining the Inflection Point: Jensen Huang’s keynote at NVIDIA GTC declared that the era of inference has arrived. Kaycee Lai clarifies that "inference" simply means actually *using* AI in production to solve business problems rather than just prepping models.
  • Operationalizing AI: While personal and entertainment AI have flourished, enterprises have been slow to operationalize. The "AI Data Factory" represents a shift where AI is no longer a single app or SaaS tool but is core to every department and data stream.
  • The "Always-On" Demand: Unlike training, which is a means to an end, inference in a factory setting runs 24/7. Modern data estates must evolve to handle a massive increase in velocity, moving from answering one question every three months to potentially thirty questions per second.

The Architecture of the AI Data Factory

  • The Factory Analogy: Kaycee Lai breaks down the components of an AI factory to move past marketing jargon:
      • Inputs: Raw data from across the enterprise.
      • The "Plumbing": Physical infrastructure including servers, networking, storage, compute, power, and space.
      • The Process: Models and applications that transform data.
      • The Output: Consumable value such as applications, data sets, or automated documentation.
  • Not a Single SKU: Despite many vendors selling "AI Factories," it is not a single product. Like a bread factory versus a car factory, every AI factory will look different based on the specific business problem it is designed to solve.
  • NVIDIA’s Blueprint: NVIDIA is positioning itself as a provider of "reference architectures"—essentially the road systems, traffic lights, and lane markers—upon which enterprises must build their own specific "vehicles."

The Rise of Autonomous Workers (CLAW)

  • Defining CLAW: An emerging concept in the industry is the Continuous Learning Autonomous Worker (CLAW)—agents that are always learning, autonomous, and capable of mimicking human-level task coordination.
  • From Single Agents to Orchestration: The industry is moving from single-threaded agents to multi-agent frameworks.
      • Nemo CLAW: NVIDIA’s framework designed to provide the governance, security, and "sniff test" required by IT departments to trust autonomous agents with corporate data and policies.
  • Agentic Workflows: Kaycee Lai describes building internal tools (e.g., "Abbie") where multiple agents coordinate to research a client, identify AI use cases, and generate tailored sales collateral in seconds based on a single prompt.
  • The Resource Impact: Agents do not sleep or take vacations. An autonomous worker continuously generating output also continuously consumes storage, networking, and GPU resources, requiring a highly resilient infrastructure.

Strategic Roadmap: Working Backward for the CIO

  • The Infrastructure Trap: Many CIOs are buying high-performance GPUs and infrastructure before identifying their specific use cases. Lai warns that the world’s most powerful "power drill" is wasted if the user doesn't know what they are building.
  • The "Hostess" Methodology: To avoid AI failure, enterprises should work backward through the supply chain:

1. Identify the "Snack": What does the customer want to buy? (The Business Outcome).

2. Source the Ingredients: What data is required to produce that outcome? (Data Readiness).

3. Build the Factory: What specific infrastructure and toolsets are needed to process that data at scale?

  • Core Business Use Cases: AI success is often found in traditional problems made faster, such as predictive maintenance in manufacturing or real-time inventory planning in supply chain management.

Notable Quotes

"Inference means you're actually using it. It's as simple as that... The technology is ready for people to actually consume inference safely and on a regular basis in production." — Kaycee Lai
"It doesn't matter how great the performance is, if you actually don't even know what the use case you're trying to solve for... you're never going to actually use the infrastructure in a way that's going to deliver value." — Kaycee Lai
"NVIDIA is providing the road system, the traffic lights, the stop signs, the lane markers, the speed limits, and then essentially asking enterprises to go put their cars on the road to go where they need to." — Shawn Rosemarin
"An efficient factory is constantly running, consuming inputs and generating outputs... Every single time I have wait time where trucks aren't getting loaded, I'm losing money." — Kaycee Lai

Insights from a 25-Year Monetary Policy Experiment | Paul Sheard

Levy Economics Institute(12.6K) ·87:39 ·40 views · 2026-04-08
Paul Sheard (Economist and Author)
Quantitative Easing (QE) has evolved from a niche Japanese experiment into a global monetary standard, fundamentally decoupling central bank balance sheet size from interest rate policy and revealing that monetary and fiscal operations are two sides of the same sovereign coin.
TL;DR Quantitative Easing (QE) has evolved from a niche Japanese experiment into a global monetary standard, fundamentally decoupling central bank balance sheet size from interest rate policy and revealing that monetary and fiscal operations are two sides of the same sovereign...

TL;DR

Quantitative Easing (QE) has evolved from a niche Japanese experiment into a global monetary standard, fundamentally decoupling central bank balance sheet size from interest rate policy and revealing that monetary and fiscal operations are two sides of the same sovereign coin.

The Structural Shift: From Scarce to Ample Reserves

The operational framework of central banking underwent a "monumental change" during the 2008 financial crisis, transitioning from a reactive model to a proactive, large-scale interventionist model.

  • Pre-2008 "Scarce Reserves" Framework: Central banks were passive, expanding balance sheets only to meet the public's demand for banknotes and minimum reserve requirements.
      • The Constraint: Because the Fed did not pay interest on reserves, it had to keep reserves scarce. Any "excess" would have forced the federal funds rate to zero.
  • The "Smoking Gun" Legislation: The 2008 TARP legislation granted the Fed authority to pay interest on reserves (IOR).
      • Decoupling: This allowed the Fed to separate interest rate decisions from balance sheet size. It could hike rates while maintaining a bloated balance sheet by simply raising the IOR.
  • The "Ample Reserves" Era: The Fed now operates with trillions in excess reserves ($2.4 trillion currently), a regime Sheard notes is operationally easier for the Fed but lacks transparency regarding what "ample" actually means.

Insights from the 25-Year Japanese "Laboratory"

Japan pioneered modern QE on March 19, 2001, in response to a decades-long deflationary spiral triggered by a massive collapse in asset prices.

  • Deflationary Context: Residential property prices fell 46% over 18 years; commercial property in large cities plummeted 80-87%.
  • QE1 vs. QQE:
      • QE1 (2001–2006): A "diffident" approach that increased the balance sheet by only 34%. Sheard argues it was too small-scale to be effective.
      • QQE (2014–2024): Under Governor Kuroda, the "Bazooka" approach involved massive Japanese Government Bond (JGB) purchases and "qualitative" easing (buying equity ETFs and REITs).
  • Current Standing: Despite exiting QQE in March 2024, the Bank of Japan still owns approximately 42% of all outstanding JGBs, rendering the idea of government "default risk" moot.

The Theory of QE as Debt Refinancing

Sheard posits that QE is best understood not as "printing money" in a vacuum, but as a sophisticated debt management operation for the "consolidated government."

  • Debt Refinancing: QE is essentially the government retiring its own interest-bearing bonds and replacing them with central bank reserves (another form of government liability).
  • The "Primitive State": QE returns the financial system to a state where the government runs a deficit by electronically "printing" money and leaving it in the system, rather than draining those reserves through bond issuance.
  • MMT Alignment: The mechanics of QE validate core Modern Monetary Theory (MMT) insights—specifically that governments create money through spending and destroy it through taxation, with bond issuance acting as a reserve-draining tool rather than a funding necessity.

The Potency and Limits of the QE Tool

While QE restored "symmetry" to the central bank toolkit, its actual impact on the real economy is often overestimated by financial journalists and underestimated in its complexity.

  • Restoring Symmetry: Central banks can always hike rates to fight inflation, but they hit a "zero bound" when cutting. QE provides a way to continue easing when interest rates cannot go lower.
  • The "Low Potency" Reality: QE is a "low potency" nudge. Because every dollar of liquidity injected is balanced by a dollar of assets removed from the private sector, the net change in private purchasing power is zero.
  • The Portfolio Rebalance Effect: QE works indirectly by forcing investors out of safe government bonds and into riskier assets (equities, corporate debt), theoretically lowering borrowing costs across the economy.
  • The Fiscal Signal: Sheard argues that if an economy reaches the point where QE is necessary, it is a "surefire sign" that monetary policy has reached its limit and aggressive fiscal policy is the required solution.

***

"QE is essentially a debt refinancing operation of the consolidated government... whereby the government retires government bonds and refinances them into central bank money, reserves. It’s just a change in the debt profile of the issuer." — Paul Sheard
"In a fiat money system, what do you repay money with? You repay it with money. If you turn [debt] into reserves, there is no maturity date... the default risk is obviously gone up in smoke." — Paul Sheard
"QE renders moot the idea that the government needs to repay its debt... reserves and government bonds are just alternative forms of government debt." — Paul Sheard
Good morning. Good morning everyone. It is my pleasure to welcome you to the Levy Economics Institute for a special lecture which officially kicks off our celebration of a major milestone, the 40th anniversary of the institute's founding. We are thrilled to have with us Dr. Paul Sheard. Paul is not just a speaker, but a valued member of our own board of advisors, and his presence here is incredibly fitting for an event that looks back at our history while engaging with some core issues of today. For those of you who may not know, the Levy Economics Institute was established in 1986 by the late Leon Levy, who was an investor and philanthropist. His founding vision was to create a place dedicated to the in-depth empirical study of financial markets and monetary policy, and in particular financial instability, which was largely ignored by the mainstream at the time. This focus was amplified by the foundational work of our two most renowned scholars, Hyman Minsky and Wynne Godley. Minsky's financial instability hypothesis and Godley's sectoral balances approach gave us the tools to understand financial fragility and the macroeconomic dynamics in a way that proved to be prophetic, ahead of the 2008 financial crisis. Over the years, we have been keenly interested in exploring the connection between financial instability and economic insecurity, and we had added research programs on economic insecurity, employment, distribution, and well-being. It is exactly because of this legacy that we are so fortunate to have Paul with us today, as he is the consummate central bank watcher. He's a veteran of financial markets and policy, having spent decades at the intersection of theory and practice. Paul's career is remarkable; he was a markets economist in Tokyo during Japan's lost decades, a front-row witness to the global financial crisis as Lehman Brothers' chief economist, and later global chief economist at both Nomura Securities and S&P Global. He has served on advisory committees for the Japanese government, has been a visiting scholar at the Bank of Japan, and is currently a senior fellow at the Harvard Kennedy School.
