It was this thing that people would come to Japan and sort of look at, not realizing that before too long, it was coming to their shores as well. So, first of all, let's start with a definition. What is quantitative easing? What do we mean by this term? I would define it, and I think this is fairly standard, as the central bank purposefully easing monetary policy by expanding its balance sheet. How does it do that? By buying assets, usually government bonds, but not always. In principle, it could be any asset, but central banks being conservative and somewhat limited usually in what they can do, they usually buy government debt securities, paid for by creating reserves. What are reserves? This is a funny word because people talk about foreign exchange reserves. This is the component of base money, if you like. It's the deposits that commercial banks have at the central bank. We'll just call them reserves. Each country sort of calls them different things, but yeah, reserves—as in Federal Reserve, it is easy to remember. So, that's what quantitative easing is. I put the word "purposefully" in there because central banks do, or before QE would, expand their balance sheet. But what happened under QE, and again I'll show a picture in a minute, it became much more proactive and purposeful and larger in scale. Somewhere along the line I lost my bottle of water. Yoshko, do you have it? Oh, maybe it is down there. Yeah, see if you could... Thanks, Pavlina. Yeah, thank you very much. So let me show you. Here's a picture of quantitative easing. This is from 2002; that's just where the data starts for this chart. This shows the size of the Federal Reserve's balance sheet up until today. You can see that over time it has ratcheted up. That first dotted line is the end of 2008. That's when quantitative easing, this thing that the Bank of Japan pioneered, came to US shores in the financial crisis. Now initially, for those of you who kind of know the history here, that increase in the balance sheet was what people called credit easing or emergency liquidity injections—different credit easing programs by the Fed in response to the collapse in the financial system. This was apropos of what Pavlina was talking about a moment ago, the global financial crisis. The Fed responded with all kinds of liquidity injections. But pretty soon from the end of 2008 moving into 2009, the Fed kind of pivoted, knowing that those liquidity operations and short-term crisis prevention measures would unwind. They wanted to keep easing monetary policy in a different way, and so they turned to quantitative easing, buying treasuries and government-guaranteed mortgage-backed securities. So you go up and up and up and plateau around 2015, a little bit right about 2015. Then, you know, it comes down a little bit. And then the next one, of course, is COVID. You'll see already, when you get to COVID, the Fed had never actually unwound the QE or the balance sheet expansion that it did in response to the global financial crisis. But now it's facing a new situation: a very sudden, dramatic drop in GDP. This could have had tremendous negative consequences beyond even the immediate impact, and so they really went to the races. They really put their foot on the accelerator and did kind of fairly massive quantitative easing, buying a lot of government Treasury bills, bonds, and mortgage-backed securities. They did that by creating essentially central bank money or base money. The central bank has the privilege of being able to create money out of thin air. Commercial banks can as well, but that's a different story. We'll leave them out of the picture for the moment. So the balance sheet of the Fed went up, hit about nine trillion dollars around the end of 2023 ish, and now it's coming down to around about six and a half trillion now. So that's a picture of quantitative easing by the Federal Reserve over time. Now, let me... excuse me. I think it's useful at this point just to mention Wynne Godley and sectoral balances. This is not exactly sectoral balances, but identities. Economists, as a footnote, classically trained economists tend to be a little bit dismissive of counting identities and things like that. It sounds too much like accounting rather than economics, but I find they come in very, very handy, particularly for understanding quantitative easing. It's very simple. So here's a simplified central bank balance sheet. On one side, the left-hand side, the asset side, you've got various assets. The principal one is usually government bonds. Now everything's more complicated; there's always a lot of footnotes. Often central banks hold these assets and increase or decrease them through repurchase operations, repo operations, but it amounts to the same thing. On the right-hand side, the liability side, there are three major components that are worth focusing on. Now this is simplified. If you looked at the actual balance sheet of any central bank, you know you'd see 15 or 20 different components, but this is basically the key analytics. The three components are reserves—the accounts or deposits that commercial banks have at the central bank, say at the Fed—banknotes, and government deposits. I always have one in my pocket when I talk about these things. This is... I usually have a twenty-dollar note. I like the twenty-dollar note because it has Andrew Jackson on it, which is a little inside joke. But this one's even better because it's got Alexander Hamilton on it. This, of course, is a banknote, so that's there on the liability side, and this is a Federal Reserve Note. It is a liability of the Federal Reserve. The Federal Reserve is part of the government, so it's a government liability as well. Sure enough, I used to play a little party trick at this point but I'm not going to do it here. I ask the audience, who do you think signs this paper money, this liability of the Federal Reserve? Of course everyone says, well the Governor of the Fed, the Fed chair, or maybe the Secretary of the Treasury. Well, Secretary of the Treasury, yes, but not the Fed chair. It's actually signed by the US Treasurer, which is a different position within the Treasury. It is just a little footnote of a footnote, but it's kind of striking that even though it's a Federal Reserve note, it's not signed by anybody at the Fed. So banknotes are becoming a rarer thing, but there's still north of two trillion dollars of banknotes that float around. Then there are government deposits, and this is often a surprise to people because of course the Fed is part of the government. But the government, particularly the Treasury on the fiscal side, has banks with its own bank, which is the Federal Reserve in the US. So bank deposits are an important part of the balance sheet of any central bank. Now being two sides of a balance sheet, you can put the two together as an identity and just rearrange. What I'm interested in is reserves, because reserves are the most fundamental form of money, and it's what central banks can create if they choose to, pretty much at will. If you look at that little delta equation, it says there are really three things that influence the amount of reserves in the system at any point in time. That is the amount of liabilities, this kind of money, that the central bank has issued. Roughly what they say is—and again you can hold two constant and look at the other just to get an idea—if you go to the bank, the ATM, and you take cash out, banknotes go up because you're increasing the amount of banknotes in circulation. That means reserves go down. So there's a reverse relationship. Ditto for government deposits. If the government, for example, takes in tax revenue, its deposits go up at the central bank, and that means reserves go down. If the government spends money, writes checks for social security, or writes a check to Lockheed for some missiles, then reserves will go up and deposits will go down. Also, if the government issues bonds, it's now collecting money from the private sector; reserves go down and deposits go up. So that's an important component, but often overlooked. The third one is really most relevant for today's discussion, which is that a change in reserves equals a change in assets if the other two components are held constant. Bear in mind, the central bank knows pretty much on a day-by-day basis what these other two components are doing. They know on a typical day how much money is going to flow in and out of the banking system because of fiscal flows and because of the public going to the bank, putting money in, or taking money out. They have a lot of experience at this. So basically, the central bank gets to be in some sense the last mover of saying, "Okay, what's happened to reserves, and where do we want the reserve level to be?" In other words, how much money do we want the banking system in aggregate to have at the Fed today? They get to determine that simply by either increasing or decreasing their assets. This is normally... economists love jargon, so we normally call this open market operations. I don't know about you, but people's eyes often glaze over at "open market operations." But it's basically the Fed buying and selling assets to change the level of reserves in the system. Okay, that's the balance sheet. But let's see how that relates to what the Fed does. Well, it turns out that that capital R, that reserve component, plays a really critical part in monetary policy. For this talk, I'm going to sort of... and I'm glad Pavlina mentioned history, because there is a history here which I'm going to go through a little bit. The role that these reserves play has kind of changed. Things have changed in the monetary policy world. How does the Fed usually operate? Well, it operates monetary policy by raising or cutting the federal funds rate. And what is the federal funds rate? Well, it's none other than the rate at which the banks that have these deposits at the Fed lend or borrow reserves from other banks. This is a little club. The banks have these reserves at the Fed and they might, on a day-to-day basis, want to lend or borrow from one another. One bank might have what they feel is too many; another bank may be short—I'll explain why they might be short in a minute—and they trade these reserves among themselves overnight. "Okay, you need a million dollars? I'll give you a million dollars, you repay it tomorrow." This is all happening on the Fed's balance sheet. That's called the federal funds rate, that interest rate at which these trades take place. So the Fed over time, when it wants to tighten monetary policy, say for example after 2022, when the interest rate was close to zero, it took it up to five and a half percent. Now it's sort of cutting back to three and a half to three and three quarters. What it's historically doing is acting on that federal funds rate, the interbank rate, the overnight interbank rate. Now, why would that work? This is where I'm going to tell the story in sort of two stages. This is the Fed's balance sheet now zooming in on the period up until around about September 2008. Remember September 2008? That's when the global financial system had its heart attack. Nothing magical about 2003; that's just where the data starts when I download from the FRED database. Now, what do you see here? You see a little bit of volatility obviously, but basically, the size of the Fed's balance sheet is just gently rising on a steady basis over those five years or so. Why is that? Here is where we come to a critical piece of institutional detail. Up until the end of 2008, the Federal Reserve did not pay interest on reserves. So those deposits that the commercial banks had at the Fed were sitting there and they did not get any interest remuneration. They were basically an electronic version of a banknote. You don't get any interest on a banknote. At least not yet. Maybe when they become digital, that will change. So why is that important? And why, under those circumstances, was the balance sheet just expanding like this? Well, if you go back to the balance sheet identity, there are three main components: reserves, banknotes, and government deposits. Over time, government deposits go up and down but they basically average out kind of close to zero, so they're not really a factor in the growth of the central bank's balance sheet over time. Because the government is typically running a budget deficit, it's issuing bonds, draining reserves, and then spending, which restores those reserves, so it's just kind of like a seesaw. So that's not a factor. The two factors that are important are, one, the public's demand for banknotes. Typically, as the economy grows, people will hold a little bit more cash and banknotes. And so that tends to move up over time. What about reserves? Here we come to another technical piece of institutional detail. Often the devil is in the detail with this stuff. That is what is called minimum reserve requirements. What minimum reserve requirements are is... again, this goes back to the founding of the Fed in 1913, triggered by the 1907 Wall Street crash and JP Morgan having to step in as the kind of lender of last resort. Policy makers realized, oh, we better have a central bank like most other countries. So they instituted the Federal Reserve. The idea of minimum reserve requirements is to say, depending on how many deposits a bank has, we're going to require that bank to hold a certain fraction, a small fraction, on deposit at the central bank. So if there's an unexpected withdrawal of deposits from the bank, there will be literally money in the bank to pay the depositors. Typically it is a small amount of money. Now why is that important? Let me try to close the circle here. It gets a little bit complicated, although many of you in this audience probably already know the story. In order to... remember the interest rate that the Fed is trying to control is the federal funds rate. It's the rate in this market for the borrowing and lending of reserves. Because you have minimum reserve requirements and zero interest on reserves, it turns out that the Fed, or any central bank operating the same system, essentially has to, on a day-by-day basis, supply the amount of reserves that equals the total minimum reserve requirements. If you went back to 2008 and put numbers on this, minimum reserve requirements at the time of the financial crisis, I think, were around 50 billion dollars. What would happen if the Fed said, "You know what, I'm in a miserly mood today. Reserves went down because of those two flows we talked about, down to 25 billion, and I'm not going to top it up"? Well, it is called "minimum required reserves" for a reason. They have to maintain them over the course of a month, not on a daily basis, but they have to meet their requirements; otherwise, they get penalized by the Fed. If there are not enough reserves in the system collectively that the banks need to satisfy the very requirements that the Fed imposed, guess what? They'll be scrambling for funds and the interest rate will go up. But the interest rate going up in the federal funds rate market contradicts the assumption that the Federal Reserve is controlling that interest rate at a certain level. What happens in the other direction? What if there are too many reserves? The Fed says, "I'm feeling in a very generous mood. The system only needs 50 billion dollars, but I'll inject 80 billion dollars." Now here's the kicker: those banks have to hold those reserves because it's the Fed that determines in aggregate the reserves in the system. But they don't get any interest rate. The banks, being profit-seeking entities, will say, "I've got too many reserves, I don't want to hold this stuff, I want to buy another asset or lend it to some other bank." Well, guess what, everybody's in the same boat. So that will lead to the interest rate collapsing. So again, it contradicts the assumption that the Fed is actually targeting the federal funds rate. In other words, it was a corollary of the fact that you had a positive interest rate, you had minimum reserve requirements, and you had no interest on reserves, that the Fed had no choice but to passively, reactively adjust the level of reserves to always be in line with minimum reserve requirements. Again, it's all very circular. I repeat: minimum reserve requirements that the Fed itself imposed on the system. Why would that go up over time? Because again, the economy is growing, typically bank lending is growing, which means the deposit base is growing, and that means minimum reserve requirements are growing. So this upward slope is just those two things happening. Prior to 2008, the Fed was very passive and reactive with its balance sheet. Guess what? Market economists didn't really pay any attention to the Fed's balance sheet. All they were interested in was what Greenspan was going to do at the next FOMC meeting, not what was happening to the Fed's balance sheet. It was the federal funds rate that was important. So that was the story. Now everything changed. I've talked about actual reserves and the minimum reserve requirements. What I just explained to you is that actual reserves up until the end of 2008 were in line with—had to be in line with—minimum reserve requirements. There's a concept called excess reserves. Excess reserves just means the amount of reserves that are actually there minus the amount that the banks need to hold in aggregate. Well, if A minus B equals zero, that's what I just explained. It was a corollary of the way the system worked. You may not realize these are data points here. That's not just the X axis; that's also the chart that I'm charting. So there's visual proof that what I've just told you about the way the system worked is actually what the data says. But then look what happens. Excess reserves go through the roof. That is just the balance sheet counterpart on the liability side to the QE that the Fed was doing. Now, I should just say very briefly, what is the point of this QE? If I went into that rabbit hole it would take another whole hour lecture, so I'm not going to go too deep. But basically, it's called quantitative easing because it's another form of monetary easing. It's essentially monetary easing that the central bank does when they've exhausted their interest rate ammunition. Go back to the financial crisis. A year before the heart attack happened, the federal funds rate was about two and three-quarters percent. Fifteen months later, it was zero. Most of that happened in that few months after the crisis. So the Fed, in reaction to the crisis, cut the interest rate to zero. But they wanted to do more than that, just like the Bank of Japan in 2001 wanted to do more as well. One thing that you can do is go into the market, buy a whole lot of government treasuries, and try to put downward pressure on interest rates not just at the short end, but across the whole yield curve. Technically, this is talked about in terms of QE taking the term premium out of the market, suppressing the term premium. Also, jargon that you'll hear is the portfolio rebalance effect. By taking government debt securities out of the market and putting in cash instead, those two assets are not exactly the same. The central bank through QE is able to disturb the aggregate portfolio of the private sector, and then the people who are running portfolios try to rebalance. They say, "I've got too much cash, I want more risk assets or more bonds," and they start reallocating. But there's a fallacy of composition, or maybe it's an adding up constraint; in aggregate, the market has to hold the portfolio that is there, and so that's a bit of a fool's errand. The idea is this will put downward pressure on interest rates, put upward pressure on asset prices, and lead to a kind of easing effect. So that's the theory behind why you would do this in the first place. When you run out of interest rate ammunition, let's do the next best thing: try to put downward pressure on interest rates across the economy. So that's kind of the second phase. Let me talk a little bit, just before I do... you'll notice here, let me introduce another concept and sort of the history. If you went back to the federal funds rate chart before, you'll see that the federal funds rate was cut to zero in the financial crisis. And then from December 2015, when Janet Yellen came in, the Fed started to raise the interest rate up again. On this chart, you can see that the balance sheet at that point was almost at a peak level. And yet the Fed started raising the federal funds rate. Now, if you're following the intricacies here, you'll say, "Hold on a minute, Paul. We've got a bloated Fed balance sheet and now the Fed is starting to raise the federal funds rate. But didn't you tell me before that reserves don't get any interest?" If you have any amount of excess reserves, let alone a huge amount in the system, you're essentially going to force the interest rate down to zero. So it's impossible for the Fed to raise the interest rate, as they did from the end of 2015, if there are excess reserves in the system—if those reserves do not get any interest. So something must have changed. In fact, something did change. What changed was in October 2008 when the TARP legislation was passed. Under that legislation, literally under the TARP, the Fed acquired the legal authority to pay interest on reserves. Actually, that was in the pipeline; legislation had been passed in 2006 which gave the Fed this power, but it was going to come in 2011. They just brought it forward to 2008. That was a bit of a moot point because the Fed didn't need the power to pay interest rates when it was doing QE and wanted the federal funds rate to be at zero; it didn't really matter. However, once it started to exit from QE and said, "We now want to move back to a positive interest rate," it needed the ability to pay interest on reserves to get around this problem. If you didn't pay interest on reserves, and banks held reserves in excess of the amount they were required to, they would want to dump those reserves and that would force the interest rate to zero. So that change, that sort of obscure legal change, had dramatic consequences for the operation of monetary policy. Again, the devil is often in the detail on these things. By the way, if you were able to look closely at this chart's footnote, you'll see that it ends in around about March 2020. In March 2020, the Fed kind of threw in the towel, recognized the reality, and announced that given there were massive excess reserves in the system, the whole idea of minimum reserve requirements was superfluous. It was kind of moot, and they set minimum reserve requirements to zero. They are still zero. They could come back, who knows, but probably not while we're in this kind of world. Let me tack back a little bit to the Bank of Japan, because this is where it all started. Here is a simple chart. This is just residential property prices for Japan, but what you see with that Mount Fuji part is a run-up in residential property prices and then a collapse from around about 1991. Property prices went down about forty-six percent over an 18-year period. Forty-six percent—it's not huge, but it's like year in, year out real estate prices falling. That's very deflationary. Now if you look at commercial property prices, it's even much worse than this. From peak to trough in the large cities in Japan, commercial property prices fell by 87 percent. This was a huge deflationary force. Fortunately, now real estate prices are trending back up again, but they're nowhere near their peak level back in 1991. That's just to give you an idea of... aha, something really big in a deflationary way happened in Japan, and of course, that was what presaged and led to the Bank of Japan's initial quantitative easing. Now what about the US? What this chart shows—one blue line, one green line—these are the GDP deflators for Japan and the US. Economists don't usually look at GDP deflators, but here it's a convenient measure. It's not what central banks directly target; they usually target the CPI or a variant in the US, the PCE. But the GDP deflator is essentially the measure of inflation for the whole economy, including investment. If you look at the green line, that's the US. This is from right about 1994 until current. It goes up and up; you can see the COVID inflation shock goes up at a steeper rate. But the GDP deflator is supposed to be positive—one percent, two percent, something like that—not negative. But you can see what Japan's so-called lost decades were all about; it was this deflation. Economy-wide, there was mild but continuous, chronic deflation that went on for the best part of 20 years. Now you can see we're back in a more normal world; the deflator in both economies is moving up again courtesy of the COVID recession. But this was what the Bank of Japan and other policy makers were trying to deal with. So let's go back to the Bank of Japan's QE. By the way, I don't have a watch in front of me, but time check, Pavlina? Dimitri, you should... I think we have plenty of time. Okay. You have five, seven minutes? Seven? Ten? Yeah, sure. Let me just sort of kind of whiz through. So this is the QE of the Bank of Japan. I want to point out a couple of things on the same chart. The first dotted line is what I call QE1. That's the first round of quantitative easing from March 2001 to March 2006, this five-year period of QE. Now what you notice is that it doesn't look very dramatic. Right. Now if you zoomed in it would be a little bit more dramatic. It's about a 34 percent increase in the size of the balance sheet of the Bank of Japan over a 5-year period. But relative to subsequent QE that we saw, not just from the Bank of Japan but from the Fed, the ECB, the Bank of England, and other central banks, it was really very small scale. Therefore, in a sense, it was not surprising that it wasn't very effective. Mechanically, the reason for that was what the Bank of Japan was doing with its QE was targeting an increase in the reserves. It took the reserves from a little bit less than five trillion yen up to 35 trillion over a five-year period. However, as we saw before, reserves are only one part of the balance sheet. If you increase a component of the balance sheet by a certain percentage, you're going to be increasing the overall balance sheet by a lesser percentage. So it wasn't very aggressive QE at all. Now the second round is QQE. This is under Governor Kuroda, started in April 2014. QQE being "quantitative and qualitative easing." The qualitative aspect gets at the point that the Bank of Japan was not just buying JGBs, Japanese government bonds, but also risk assets. They were particularly buying equity ETFs on quite a decent scale, as well as, on a smaller scale, JREITs—real estate investment trusts. This actually made sense because if you go back to the basic theory of QE, if you're going to do QE, two things suggest themselves: you really want to do it on a big scale, bazooka style, to really have an impact on psychology and also to try to amplify these portfolio rebalance effects. If the central bank is unilaterally disturbing the composition of the aggregate portfolio held by the private sector, you want to be taking an asset out of the system that is quite different from, or a poor substitute for, the asset that you're putting into the portfolio in its place. If you did it with perfect substitutes, then there'd be no effect by definition. So if you want to get a bang for the buck, you want to choose an asset that is as different as possible from the asset that you're supplying. You are supplying central bank money, and you take out equities. Now you might get a little bit more juice from that portfolio rebalance. That was the idea behind QQE. It ended in March 2024 when the Bank of Japan, under Governor Ueda, was able to say, "You know, we think we've reached mission accomplished." But the reality was, this is largely, probably entirely... no, that's too strong a term... largely due to the COVID inflation shock. The Bank of Japan was really the only central bank in the world for which the COVID inflation shock was somewhat welcomed. Although the Japanese public doesn't see it that way; they didn't particularly like the idea of prices going up. So that's kind of the Bank of Japan's experience. You can see two very distinct phases: very low-key, very diffident, and not very aggressive, followed by a major policy shift under Governor Kuroda where the Bank of Japan really sort of drank the Kool-Aid on QE. Which, in some sense, had been ironic. The Bank of Japan invented QE but was the least aggressive until Governor Kuroda. They said, "No, we invented this stuff, we know how to do it, we're gonna go for it." I was very supportive of QQE and very critical of the way the Bank of Japan was doing QE1, but again, it's easy for an armchair analyst to make these calls. I want to end with five quick slides because I think we're running out of time. I want to have lots of QA... QE, QA... I want to have lots of Q&A. Freudian slip. Just some insights. What lessons can we draw from this experience of QE for a quarter of a century now? Well, the first one is just a kind of analytical insight, which is that the way to think about QE conceptually is... what is this? You can describe it in various ways. You can talk about the portfolio rebalance effect, you can talk about printing money, and you can talk about large-scale asset purchases. But essentially what it is, is a debt refinancing operation of the consolidated government. Consolidated government meaning the government including the central bank. The government retires government bonds and refinances them into central bank money and reserves. So it's just a change in the debt profile of the issuer. If you're familiar with corporate finance, you might start thinking of the Modigliani-Miller theorem or something like that, where you're just changing the nature of the debt profile. When you think about it a little bit more carefully, what QE is doing is a sort of toggling. Central bank money, reserves, and government bonds are just two variants of the same thing. They're a government liability, but they take a slightly different form and have different institutional characteristics. There's a toggling going on, because if you go back to the more primitive state, you can think of what goes on when the government runs a budget deficit in a more fundamental way. The government runs a deficit and it's injecting money into the system. It's creating reserves. But typically, in a modern system, the government wants to change those reserves into government bonds. So it issues government bonds, which then reduces the... budget deficit increases reserves, and issuing government bonds decreases reserves, dollar for dollar. Now QE comes along and the central bank buys those reserves back. That is, the government buys its own bonds back through the central bank, and it just creates those reserves again. So QE in some sense is like returning to this more primitive state of nature, where you don't bother with government bond issuance, you just run the deficit and, in some sense, electronically print the money. The money being the base money. And you just leave it in the system. So that's essentially what it is. And then when we get to QT, it just restores it again, so you go back to issuing bonds and the private sector holding bonds. The other thing related to this is that in some sense QE, at least the way I've always thought about it, kind of lifts the veil and reveals just how intertwined monetary and fiscal policy are. It shows how intertwined monetary and fiscal operations are through the balance sheet of the central bank. It leads you to many of what I think you would say are the MMT insights, the sort of stuff I've been talking about: that governments actually create money when they spend. They destroy money when they tax. Governments don't really need to borrow money because they create money. Now, institutionally they do borrow money and issue debt securities, but that's really done to drain those reserves from the system. So this is how I came into the MMT kind of world—literally sitting in Tokyo trying to figure this stuff out, and trying to come into some of these understandings. I started to read stuff and discovered Wynne Godley and Randy Wray and Warren Mosler and Pavlina and other people who write in this area. I think this is a huge takeaway from QE. It kind of reveals that MMT is very correct with many of its insights around the way the plumbing of the financial system works. Why is that important? Because in the markets, with central bank independence, which is there for a reason, we tend to talk about the central bank over here—the Fed—as independent. It's not really thought of as part of the government. We have this thing called fiscal policy over there. But I would posit that many, if not most, financial market participants, financial journalists, public policy people, politicians, and general citizenry don't realize those two things are really two sides of the same coin and intricately connected. So that's a great thing. The second one is just this fundamental change that's happened, and there's the combination of QE and interest on reserves. Generally speaking, I bang on about this stuff, but I don't see a lot of people in market circles making this connection: that central banking has changed fundamentally. Because of QE and interest on reserves, the central banks now can separate their interest rate decision from their balance sheet decision. They now have two independent tools: the size and composition of the balance sheet and the interest rate. That gives them enormous degrees of freedom, and that's why the Fed has got a balance sheet of six and a half trillion dollars rather than, say, two trillion dollars, which it might otherwise have had to have. Again, it all comes back to this point that if central banks want to do QE, they absolutely need to be able to pay interest on reserves. It doesn't matter in the phase in which they're easing monetary policy because they want a zero interest rate. But if they're not able to pay interest on reserves when they got around to wanting to raise the interest rate, they would first have to go through this period of shrinking the balance sheet back to the point where there were essentially no more excess reserves in the system. That's going to put a check on their willingness to be aggressive in the QE phase, because they're going to say, "Once we finish QE, we're not going to be able to raise interest rates for some period of time. We're going to be held hostage to the QE." Unless we can detach and separate the QE decision from the interest rate decision. The simple thing of paying interest on reserves... you'll hear stories like, "Oh no, what that does is stop banks from lending their reserves out and fueling inflation." That's nonsense. We know from the balance sheet identity that whatever the amount of aggregate reserves in the system is, the banks have to hold it. They don't have the ability to lend out those reserves. But you hear all these stories. I think it's much simpler than that. Now the Fed describes this in terms of going from a... again, economists love obfuscating jargon... they describe this in terms of moving from a scarce reserves framework to an ample reserves framework. In December, the Fed said, "You know, we brought the balance sheet down, but there's still about 2.3 trillion dollars or 2.4 trillion dollars of reserves in the system." Don't call them excess reserves anymore. That's an ample amount of reserves, and that's where we're going to stop. Now we're going to start expanding the balance sheet slowly over time as the demand for banknotes increases. This is called an ample reserves framework. One thing that happened under cover of darkness is we went from that old system, where you had virtually no excess reserves, to this new system where the central bank has this degree of freedom. I'm not saying if that is a good or bad thing, but it is a monumental change in the history of modern central banking which I don't think central banks are crying from the rooftops. They just want to say, "Yeah, we've moved to an ample reserves framework," and your eyes kind of gloss over at that point. Oh, ample reserves, okay. But what is ample? One of the ironies to me here is that, yes, operationally this is much easier for the central banks to operate now. They don't have to worry about tracking the reserves and making sure on a daily basis that they are just the right amount. But central banking in its modern form is supposed to be all about transparency and communication. That's the whole idea of the inflation target framework: a clear message. Now you have the situation where the Fed wakes up one morning and says, "Yeah, the amount of reserves is ample." But there's no real clear explanation about how much, theoretically, is ample. Why did you stop at 2.3 trillion? What's wrong with 2 trillion or 1.5 trillion? If you wanted to be more cynical about this, or if you're in a Fed-bashing mode, you might say this is some kind of excuse for the Fed to just maintain a bigger balance sheet presence in the economy. I just don't think that perhaps has got the attention that it deserves. Another insight that I think is quite important from an inflation expectations management viewpoint, which is a cornerstone idea in central banking these days, is that what QE did was to restore symmetry to the toolkit of the central bank. What do I mean by that? Monetary policy, at least as conventionally understood, is pretty simple. You say the central bank either has to tighten financial conditions or ease financial conditions. How does it tighten financial conditions? I know Warren Mosler doesn't agree with this, but you hike the interest rate. There's no limit on how far you could hike the interest rate. Go back to Volcker in the 1980s; if ten percent's not enough, fifteen percent. If fifteen percent's not enough to kill inflation, let's try twenty. If you're trying to influence the market's expectations and say, "I'm the central bank, I want to convince you the public that I have the tools to kill inflation," you just hike interest rates. You have that. But in the other direction, you pretty quickly hit the zero bound. And the asymmetry there is, well, once you hit the zero bound, do you have any more tools? If you don't have QE, you're basically saying that's it. Well, what if you're in a very deflationary environment like Japan was? Deflationary expectations will take root because the public will say the Bank of Japan is telling us they don't have any tools, and for whatever reason we can't rely on fiscal policy. So at least QE restored this symmetry. There's no limit on the central bank's ability, in theory at least—there might be legal limits—but to expand its balance sheet. The Federal Reserve got its balance sheet to 9 trillion dollars. If that wasn't enough, what about 12? What about 30? There's 35, 36, 37 trillion dollars of treasuries out there. Buy them all. Yeah. What about equities? Buy a bunch of equities. No limit. However, having said that, well, that's all well and good; that's a nice theoretical point. But that doesn't mean that QE is going to be very potent. In fact, I would argue, and there's a lot of empirical evidence that I don't have time to go into, that QE is a very low-potency form of monetary easing. Now some people get tricked because they hear the financial journalists talking about the Fed going back to QE, the Fed pumping trillions of dollars of liquidity into the market, or the Fed printing trillions of dollars of money. Yeah, but they're not telling you that for every dollar that they're injecting into the economy, they're pulling a dollar out in the assets that they buy. So the net purchasing power that is injected through QE is of course zero. I sort of use the term that QE is just like any monetary policy: it's more like a nudge policy trying to ease financial conditions and get people to spend or invest. But it's very indirect. On top of that, it's almost a theorem that the circumstances under which you're likely to get to a QE world are the very circumstances where QE, and monetary policy more generally, is not working. That is, it is a debt deleveraging environment. Easing financial conditions a little bit more, or trying to, is like leading a horse to water but it's not going to drink. So there's a kind of a conundrum there. What's the bottom line? The bottom line to me is, and has always been, if we get to a world of QE, that's a surefire sign that what you really need to be doing is using fiscal policy aggressively, as your main stimulus and demand management tool. Okay, two more. The other insight which sort of goes back to the first point, the sort of insights from MMT that I think QE reveals, is that QE renders moot the idea that the government needs to repay its debt. We keep hearing about the 36 or 37 trillion dollars of government debt that is out there which has to be repaid. Well, if you understand that reserves and government bonds are just alternative forms of government debt, and that the central bank, being part of the government, at will, if it wants to, can turn one into the other, you realize that that that that goes up in smoke. This is because reserves are just an electronic version of this, and this, being money, never has to be repaid. That is, we're in a fiat money system, and in a fiat money system, what do you repay money with? You repay it with money. So there's a key difference when the central bank does QE. Although it's just this debt refinancing operation, it's refinancing from one instrument that has attached to it a repayment date, a maturity date, that makes it look like it's real debt that has to be repaid. It rarely is; it's just refinanced all the time. The only way to repay government debt is to run a government surplus, and that is a rare event. So it's always being rolled over and refinanced, just because it's fiat money. But if you turn it into reserves, then it's clear: there is no maturity date on reserves. And, you know, Exhibit A here I guess is the Bank of Japan, to come back to Japan. Where even today, after the Bank of Japan has started to raise interest rates, stopped doing QQE, and exited from that regime, it still owns about 42% of all the JGBs in issue. And in theory, it could own a hundred percent. If it did, using the reductio ad absurdum argument, where is this default risk? It's obviously gone up in smoke. So I think that's an insight as well. And the last one, I just wanted to zoom out a little bit here to the last kind of takeaway or insight from the QE experience. It sort of underscores the fact that the macroeconomic policy framework—you know monetary policy, fiscal policy, throw in financial stability policy, you could throw in other things like employment policy—this framework that exists is in constant evolution. It is being subject to shocks and stresses, and therefore, and then innovations, and there's also peer learning going on from country to country, central bank to central bank, etc. And so if you look at this 25-year sweep, a lot has changed. If we just look at the monetary policy toolkit, we've talked today all about QE. I mentioned credit easing. We have a thing called forward guidance. By the way, forward guidance was part of the original, cornerstone part of the original QE framework the Bank of Japan implemented in March 2001. We have yield curve control, which the Bank of Japan pioneered from 2016. That is explicit targeting of the longer-term interest rates—10 years in Japan's case, three years in Australia's case. We have negative interest rates. The Bank of Japan didn't pioneer negative interest rates, but it joined the bandwagon pretty quickly. We have macroprudential policy as well. So these are a pretty long list of ways in which just the monetary policy framework has changed in 25 years. And if we just look at QE, you know, it's what was interesting to me, having sort of cut my teeth in Japan on this and then coming to the US and being at Lehman, watching the financial crisis unfold. I was watching Bernanke, who had lectured the Japanese on the fact that there is a printing press that can be used. You know, QE went from the Bank of Japan to the Fed, then it went to the Bank of England, eventually the ECB, back to the Bank of Japan, then pretty much with COVID to every major central bank on the planet. So you can see this process of peer learning. So the point I just want to make here is that we should lift our horizons a little bit, and of course now we have cryptocurrencies and everything else. But it's absolutely clear to me that the current framework cannot be the steady state framework that we're going to have in 25 or 50 years' time. It's going to continue to evolve, and in some sense, it's up for grabs, which makes for an interesting sort of policy research agenda as well. That's my last one. Just a very quick word on quantitative tightening if I can, I'll try to get this down to one minute. You know, in principle, that is quantitative tightening. Now QT is talked about, although we saw with the Fed before, they stopped their quantitative tightening. Now they're operating this ample reserves regime, but they never wanted to really call it tightening. So in theory, you could say well QT should be just the obverse, the other side of the coin, just change the sign. And that's kind of true; you would expect the portfolio rebalance effect just to work the other way, etc. But it's very interesting. Central banks didn't want to and don't want to look at it that way. Suddenly the quantitative easing that they did, when it now comes to the removal of the extraordinary monetary accommodation phase, let's just call that the tightening phase. You know, often central banks are saying "Oh, we're not tightening, we're just removing the accommodation," but let's just call it tightening. They don't do the first phase of tightening through quantitative tightening. They were doing the last phase of their easing through quantitative easing. It might be logical to say "well, the first thing we'll do is tighten through quantitative tightening and do the interest rate hikes later." They didn't do that. They wanted to put the unwind of QE, that quantitative tightening, sort of on the back burner, in the background. Just let it kind of happen very slowly, predictably, passively. They suddenly say, "We're no longer using this as a monetary policy tool. It's just something that we're kind of technically unwinding." I find that quite interesting, looking a little bit maybe, I don't know, "dishonest" might be too strong a word, but there's something that doesn't quite add up there. And we've seen before earlier there's kind of what I'd call a ratchet effect with QE. That once you go down this QE path, it's very hard to sort of go back to where you started. And if you look, for example, again just to come to the Federal Reserve's ample reserves regime. What they announced in December, it was a second time. The first time was I think January 2019, where they said we're now moving to an ample reserves regime. Well, reserves at the time were about 1.3 trillion dollars. Now they're about 2.4 trillion. So just in that, you know, whatever five or six years, there's been a sort of a ratchet up of now what is ample is considerably more—more than a trillion dollars more than what was judged to be ample before. And I'll just wrap with the last point I make: that maybe there's something like a Lucas critique going on here as well. That once you start using these things as policy tools, they almost start to lose their effectiveness as policy tools. But that's something maybe to throw out for the grad students to chew over and maybe come up with some fancy model that explains that. So thank you very much for your attention and I'd love to take some questions. [Moderator] Yes, we have some questions. [Audience Member] Thank you so much for the presentation. Yeah, so that's a good, very good point, and there is a kind of a literature developed now. Different economists, I mean, if you if you change the facts on the ground, economists will then have a nice research paradigm. So you know, there are papers out there that try to sort of explain what would be the benefits of the central bank having a much bigger balance sheet. And so you'll get into arguments that well, maybe risk-free assets are scarce. And so if the central bank is doing a lot of, or has done, a huge stock of QE... By the way, one thing that I didn't mention today is the stock and flow debate. When the question of like how does QE work arises, having been adjacent to the trading floor for many years doing QE, the way traders and the investment community tends to think about QE is as a flow. "Oh, the Fed is in bidding for treasuries, that's putting a bid into the market." As if the Treasury is just another participant. [Pavlina] Yeah, a follow-up on this. Isn't there a change in legal authority? The kind of assets the central banks are allowed to purchase. So are you saying that there is a change in legal authority in terms of the assets that they can purchase on an ongoing basis? Not necessarily, Pavlina. That there has been, but that's an interesting question that sort of puts the spotlight on the fact that in different countries, central banks can operate under different legal frameworks. And so, you know, notice that I'm often toggling between talking about the Fed and central banks because I tend to look at central banks more broadly, not just the Fed. And again, go to the Bank of Japan. So again, most central banks are supposed to just buy government bills, government bonds, and there might be some other kind of trade bills, discounted trade bills, but sort of stuff like that. But then there's Article 33 that says something like the Bank of Japan must restrict itself to these assets that are enumerated, unless it obtains the permission of the Prime Minister and the Minister of Finance. Now they don't do options on real estate on Mars, but you know, equities, real estate, etc. And again, from a theoretical point of view, where I would land on this is to say, well look, if you're gonna do this stuff, that's the way to do it. Now come to the Fed. Now the Fed is interesting because they're basically limited to government-issued securities. However, in terms of legal change, the one that I would point to is Section 13(3) of the Federal Reserve Act. And that's the "unusual and exigent circumstances" clause. By the way, the Federal Reserve Act is an antique, antiquated, impenetrable piece of legislation. It's pretty much as it was, but worse, than 1913. I would recommend the Bank of Japan Act. Again, that was overhauled in 1997, came into effect in 1998, and it's written pretty much in clear English. It sets out a very clear logical governance framework for how monetary policy is supposed to operate within the broader framework. And I think it would be a great thing if you could revise the Federal Reserve Act from the viewpoint of all the knowledge that we've gained and come up with something that was like more robust and more sensible. However, nobody wants to go near that with a 40-foot barge pole. They don't want to lift the—I'm sorry I'm mixing my metaphors—they don't want to let the genie out of the bottle. And so they never do that. But, of course, Dodd-Frank did in important ways change the legal framework. Which is Section 13(3). And basically what the key thing there is that the Fed now, when it does this extraordinary work, like some of these credit easing programs, lending to Main Street, or buying sort of risk assets, has to get the permission of the Secretary of the Treasury. And the COVID money, in particular, some of the COVID money was sort of allocated to Fed operations by way of the Treasury saying, "Go ahead and do this stuff, we'll give you permission to do this more risky crisis management credit operations, and we'll backstop you with the COVID money." So that is something that's happened. You know there's always a debate like if there was a real emergency in the US—and again I think something like that would happen—there's probably some loopholes in the Federal Reserve Act that you could squeeze out, where if the Treasurer said go ahead, we'll backstop you, the Fed could do quite dramatic stuff. I'm still flailing around for a good theory of exchange rate determination after doing economics for nearly half a century; I gave up there sometime. But you know, what is very interesting and notable is that from mid... so the Fed started tightening and raising interest rates, was it March 2023, do I have that right? And the Bank of Japan exited QQE a year later, but you know, they've got the interest rate then last year up to 75 basis points, but the Fed went to 5.5% from zero to five and a half percent in short order. And so you will see if you just look at the yen-dollar rate, it really went from like 115 to 155, and it's still around that area. So this major, this big move in this period in which the interest rate differential was opening up dramatically between the US and Japan. So you could say that, yes, it was monetary policy driving that. On the one hand, the ability to raise interest rates because the economy was in a much stronger position in the US, but the inability of the Bank of Japan to still normalize its monetary policy. But you know, interest rate differentials don't really explain exchange rates very well because what theory would suggest is you get a depreciation followed by... you know, uncovered interest rate parity, there should be a sort of a steady appreciation, although the data doesn't seem to support that. So how exactly... what I'm trying to get at is just looking at this as an observer over many years, it seems that moods take over foreign exchange markets. And you know, you can have the same explanation—for example, a large interest rate differential—which doesn't really move the yen versus the dollar in any particular way, and then suddenly we get into a phase where "Oh, now that explanation works." But you know, an explanation that only works after the event every now and again is not much of an explanation. So. One more question. [Audience Member] Yeah, you mentioned in a contrast like the Fed taking authority to pay interest on excess reserves in October 2008, but it was already in the pipeline since 2006. Why was it already in the pipeline? What was the institutional vulnerability? Yeah, that's a very good question. I mean, yeah, I mean, was the Fed foreseeing this? I mean, I don't know, they were extremely prescient. There has been this long-running debate in monetary economics. It goes back to Friedman—everything goes back to Milton Friedman or a lot of stuff goes back to Milton Friedman—that reserves should pay interest. So I think that Friedman's argument was essentially that not paying interest on reserves is like a tax. So the central bank is requiring the banking system to hold reserves, not paying an interest rate; it's a tax. And you know, taxes are going to have distortions. And so the argument would be, and I think Friedman made this argument, that this also leads banks to sort of game the system and try to avoid issuing liabilities that would attract minimum reserve requirements, and you just introduce distortions. So he was always of the view: just let them pay interest on reserves. Force them to hold them, but compensate for it. So maybe that eventually won the day in the Federal Reserve system, but I suspect it was probably more operational—that the Fed understood that it would be just much easier to operate its operations if the New York Fed had that authority. And then I think what happened was, so it was coming, they got that, and then of course when the TARP literally... they put it in the TARP. And I think at the time I was one of the few people who noticed that and I wrote a... I said "Oh, this is interesting." I actually read the whole TARP legislation for my sins; people thought I was crazy but I thought this is really important stuff, I better find out what's in it. But they got it into the TARP, but they... you know, again, I thought of course they realized "We want to do QE, this is going to... we need this on the way out." Just let's get it. You know, a bird in the hand is worth two in the bush, who knows what would happen through the financial crisis. If the government came back and said, "You know what, we did put that into legislation, we're now taking it back again." So they just like, bird in the hand is worth two in the bush, they wanted to get it done. I actually asked John Williams about this at a—this is on the public record—at a Council on Foreign Relations meeting late last year in New York. Just, I posed the question like, "This seems to me to be a big deal, this big change, could you tell us a little bit more about the Fed's thinking?" And he mentioned Milton Friedman and he also mentioned it's just so much easier to operate. Because you know, I don't know if this is a good reason or not, like hey everyone's paid to do a job, but if the reserves are fluctuating on a day-to-day basis and the Fed has to always make compensating moves, that's a lot of work, right, that goes into that. If you pay interest on reserves it doesn't matter anymore, so we can close those jobs down and make life easier for ourselves. So you know, that's sort of the Fed's... that seems to be their rationale. It just makes it, you know, Friedman said it was a good idea, we agree with that, and this just makes running the monetary system much much easier. But again, it does give, it opens up the doorway for a Federal Reserve that is much much more, you know, interventionist if you like, or plays a much bigger role in the economy than it would otherwise do. By the way, I brought some books like a reward for the students or anybody who would like a copy. I don't know Pavlina if I could... I gave you one, but I've got a pen and a few books here so you know if you want to get a copy of the book please run up. We'd love to have one for the Levy Library for sure, but thank you very much. Thank you for coming today. Very, very interesting talk. Thanks. Thank you very much.

White House press secretary Karoline Leavitt holds a briefing with reporters — 4/8/2026

TL;DR President Trump has declared a two-week ceasefire following the 38-day "Operation Epic Fury," which the White House claims successfully dismantled Iran’s military infrastructure and senior leadership. High-level negotiations led by Vice President JD Vance will begin this...

TL;DR

President Trump has declared a two-week ceasefire following the 38-day "Operation Epic Fury," which the White House claims successfully dismantled Iran’s military infrastructure and senior leadership. High-level negotiations led by Vice President JD Vance will begin this weekend in Islamabad to seek a long-term diplomatic solution.

Military Outcomes of Operation Epic Fury

  • Operational Success: Press Secretary Karoline Leavitt reported that the 38-day operation exceeded its core objectives, destroying Iran’s defense industrial base and its ability to manufacture or distribute weapons to regional proxies.
  • Target Statistics: The U.S. military struck more than 13,000 targets across Iran.
      • Ballistic Missiles & Drones: 450 strikes on ballistic missile units and 800 strikes on drone launching and storage facilities.
      • Naval Annihilation: Over 150 naval vessels were destroyed; Iran now possesses zero operational submarines and has lost 97% of its naval mine inventory.
      • Air Dominance: The Iranian Air Force is "operationally irrelevant," with daily flights dropping from 100 per day to zero.
  • Leadership Casualties: U.S. strikes targeted command and control structures over 2,000 times, resulting in the death of senior leaders, including Supreme Leader Ayatollah Ali Khamenei.
  • U.S. Casualties: The White House confirmed and honored 13 American service members killed during the operation.

Ceasefire Terms and the "Islamabad Talks"

  • Negotiation Framework: After discarding an initial "unserious" 10-point plan from Iran, the Trump administration accepted a modified proposal as a "workable basis" for a 15-point U.S. agreement.
  • U.S. Negotiating Team: President Trump is dispatching Vice President JD Vance, Special Envoy Steve Witkoff, and Jared Kushner to Islamabad, Pakistan, for in-person talks starting Saturday.
  • The "Red Line": Leavitt emphasized that the total end of Iranian uranium enrichment remains a non-negotiable requirement for any permanent deal.
  • Strait of Hormuz: A primary condition of the ceasefire is the "free, safe, and immediate" reopening of the Strait.
      • Tolling Proposals: The President has floated the idea of a joint U.S.-Iranian venture to charge tolls for passage, though the immediate priority is unrestricted access.
      • Conflicting Reports: While Iranian state media claimed the Strait remained closed, the White House reported an uptick in traffic and expects Iran to honor its private commitment to reopen it.

Regional and International Relations

  • Israel and Lebanon: Prime Minister Netanyahu has expressed support for the ceasefire. However, the administration clarified that Lebanon is not included in this specific truce, despite ongoing Israeli strikes in the country.
  • NATO Criticism: Leavitt delivered a harsh critique of NATO allies, stating they "failed" by not supporting the U.S. during the 38-day conflict. President Trump is scheduled to meet with NATO Secretary General Mark Rutte to discuss the future of the alliance.
  • China’s Role: The administration acknowledged high-level coordination with President Xi Jinping’s government in facilitating the ceasefire; President Trump plans to visit China in the coming weeks.
  • Future Targets: The White House signaled that the Cuban regime may be the next focus of "maximum pressure" diplomatic or economic efforts, describing the government in Havana as being in a "very weak position."

Domestic Policy and Legislation

  • The "Take It Down" Act: The White House announced the first conviction under this landmark legislation, credited to First Lady Melania Trump. The act targets non-consensual AI-generated explicit images and cyberstalking.
  • Senior Tax Relief: Leavitt highlighted the "Working Families Tax Cut," claiming it has eliminated Social Security taxes for nearly 90% of American seniors.
  • Iranian Deportations: The administration confirmed the ongoing deportation of relatives of Iranian officials (including the niece of the late General Soleimani) found to be in the U.S. on "fraudulent" asylum or visa claims.

***

Notable Quotes

"Iran's ability to build and stockpile ballistic missiles and long-range drones has also been set back by years compared to where it was six weeks ago... the Iranian Navy was completely annihilated." — Karoline Leavitt
"It’s quite sad that NATO turned their backs on the American people over the course of the last six weeks when it’s the American people who have been de-funding their defense." — Karoline Leavitt
"The President’s red lines, namely the end of Iranian enrichment in Iran, have not changed. And the idea that President Trump would ever accept an Iranian wishlist as a deal is completely absurd." — Karoline Leavitt
Good afternoon, everybody. Good to see all of you here today. Before I discuss the ceasefire with Iran, I have a quick update from the First Lady of the United States. Yesterday marked the very first conviction under the Take It Down Act. This is landmark legislation that First Lady Melania Trump played an instrumental role in getting passed. It protects victims from non-consensual AI-generated sexually explicit images, cyberstalking, and threats of violence. This is a huge achievement for the First Lady, and I know the President is very proud of his wife's efforts in getting this critical legislation passed to protect America's youth. From the very beginning of Operation Epic Fury, President Trump stated this would be a four-to-six-week military operation to dismantle the military threat posed by the radical Islamic Iranian regime. Thanks to the unbelievable capabilities of America's warfighters, the United States has achieved and exceeded those core military objectives in just 38 days. The US military destroyed Iran's defense industrial base, crushing the regime's ability to manufacture weapons that they and their proxies used to maim and kill Americans and terrorize the world. Iran's ability to build and stockpile ballistic missiles and long-range drones has also been set back by years compared to where it was six weeks ago, prior to the launch of Operation Epic Fury. We destroyed the vast majority of Iran's ballistic missiles, launcher vehicles, and long-range attack drones through more than 450 strikes on ballistic missiles and approximately 800 strikes on Iran's drone launching units and storage facilities. In total, more than 13,000 targets across Iran were struck. Again, this is in the matter of just 38 days. Meanwhile, the Iranian Navy was completely annihilated. The United States destroyed more than 150 naval vessels in total, including 16 entire classes of Iranian warships. Despite once being the largest undersea force in the Middle East, Iran now has zero submarine vessels. 97 percent of Iran's once-massive inventory of more than 5,000 naval mines has also been targeted and destroyed. Iran's Air Forces are functionally and operationally irrelevant at this point after the United States maintained total air dominance over their country for weeks on end. To underscore the significance of this, before Operation Epic Fury, the Iranian Air Force would fly between 30 to 100 flights per day. Today, that number is zero. Iran's ability to fund and support its terrorist proxies has been greatly reduced. At this point, Iran can no longer distribute weapons to its proxies in the region. Through these weapons and its navy, Iran was attempting to build a military buildup around their country that would pose an imminent and existential threat. This buildup threatened United States military assets in the Middle East, our allies in the region, and ultimately, the free world. Iran was pursuing this dangerous and aggressive strategy for one reason: to hold the entire world hostage to its terrorist ambitions. Market Relief After US-Iran Ceasefire Before Trump Deadline | The Opening Trade 4/8/2026

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·96:04 ·11.6K views · 2026-04-08
> "I like to say 'three is the new two' when it comes to long-term inflation rates.
TL;DR: Markets experienced a massive relief rally after the US and Iran agreed to a surprise two-week ceasefire just 90 minutes before a Trump-imposed deadline, sending Brent crude tumbling 14% and European equities surging over 5%. While the immediate risk of escalation has...

TL;DR: Markets experienced a massive relief rally after the US and Iran agreed to a surprise two-week ceasefire just 90 minutes before a Trump-imposed deadline, sending Brent crude tumbling 14% and European equities surging over 5%. While the immediate risk of escalation has subsided, significant uncertainty remains regarding the "maximalist" demands of both nations and the logistical complexities of reopening the Strait of Hormuz to over 800 trapped vessels.

Market Reaction and Volatility

  • Crude Oil Plunge: Brent crude dropped more than 14% at the open, falling below the $100/barrel mark for the first time in weeks as the immediate threat to Iranian infrastructure evaporated.
  • Equity Surge: The Euro Stoxx 50 rose 5.3%, while the DAX gained over 5.2% due to its high sensitivity to energy costs.
      • Sector Winners: Technology and Luxury saw sharp rebounds; ASML rose 7.6%, while Travel & Leisure gained 7.4% on hopes of lower fuel costs.
      • Sector Losers: Energy was the only sector in negative territory, with Shell down 7% and BP down 8% following the oil price collapse.
  • Bond Market Relief: European bond yields saw "staggering" moves. Italian 10-year BTP yields dropped 34 basis points, and German Bunds fell 18 basis points as markets priced out aggressive rate hikes from the ECB.
  • Hedge Fund Fallout: Recent volatility has severely impacted leveraged macro funds. Jupiter’s fund lost 12% in March, and Brevan Howard’s master fund fell 6.6%, its worst monthly loss in over 20 years.

Geopolitical Standoff and the Strait of Hormuz

  • The Ceasefire Agreement: Mediated by Pakistan with reported influence from China and US Vice President JD Vance, the deal provides a 14-day window for further negotiations in Islamabad.
  • Logistical Bottlenecks: Approximately 800 vessels remain trapped in the Persian Gulf.
      • Coordination Challenges: Iranian Foreign Minister Abbas Araghchi stated that passage through the Strait would require "coordination with Iran’s armed forces," suggesting Tehran may still exert sovereignty and potentially impose tolls.
      • Throughput: While the Strait can handle 100+ ships a day, analysts estimate it will take at least a week to clear the current backlog, assuming no technical or insurance-related delays.
  • Sustained Demands: Iran is maintaining "maximalist" demands, including the removal of US bases from the region and war reparations. Meanwhile, Israeli Prime Minister Netanyahu clarified that the ceasefire with Iran does not necessarily apply to operations against Hezbollah in Lebanon.

Long-term Macro and Central Bank Outlook

  • Inflation Narrative: Joachim Klement of Panmure Liberum suggests that "three is the new two" for long-term inflation. While the energy shock may be transitory, deglobalization and defense spending will keep structural inflation higher.
  • Federal Reserve Stance: New York Fed President John Williams indicated that monetary policy is currently "well-positioned" and favors a wait-and-see approach. He lowered his 2026 growth forecast to 2–2.5% but noted the labor market remains stable at 4.3% unemployment.
  • Real Assets: Evy Hambro of BlackRock noted that gold and copper remain attractive as "geopolitical insurance." He argued that even if peace holds, governments will continue to spend heavily on defense and energy independence, providing a floor for commodity prices.

Corporate and Industry Highlights

  • Apple (AAPL): Bloomberg reports that Apple is on track to launch its first foldable phone in September 2026, alongside the iPhone 18 Pro and Pro Max models.
  • Private Credit: Moody’s cut the outlook for Blue Owl Capital’s flagship fund to negative, citing significantly higher redemption requests as investors grow concerned over lax lending standards.
  • Aviation: IATA Director General Willie Walsh welcomed the ceasefire but warned that jet fuel and ticket prices would likely remain elevated due to the lingering "risk premium" and infrastructure damage in the Middle East.
"I like to say 'three is the new two' when it comes to long-term inflation rates. The reasons are manifold... higher risk premiums around energy, demographic trends, and the falling away of the disinflationary impact of globalization." — Joachim Klement, Head of Strategy at Panmure Liberum
"The conflict is paused... this is a war that nobody was winning. But I still remain cautious because Iran’s demands are really quite extreme and it’s hard to see them coming out having achieved everything." — Sanam Vakil, Director of Middle East and North Africa Program at Chatham House
"To my mind, monetary policy today is really well-positioned... exactly where it needs to be. We can respond if the situation changes, but right now we can wait and see." — John Williams, President of the New York Federal Reserve

Iran knows it has its leverage and can always exert that leverage. This will all depend on what kind of guarantees Iran obtains from the United States. Is this ceasefire going to become permanent? That is one of the most important demands that they're asking for. They do not want to see a repeat of this war. Secondly, what they are also looking for is so-called compensation or sanctions relief or access to their frozen assets. If Iran has economic lifelines, that can help lower the demands on the Strait of Hormuz. Iran is going to be isolated in the region. There is no return for Iran to the ties it had with its neighbors, particularly on the Gulf Arab side specifically, so that's important to remember. And if those conditions—if Iran is extracting a toll on the Strait of Hormuz—that is going to perpetually harden Gulf Arab views of the Islamic Republic. Good morning to Sanam. Can I ask a bit more about the different factions within Iran? The hardline factions versus the reformists, to the extent that it's possible to identify who's in charge of those right now. As Joumanna was telling us, the speaker Ghalibaf on the Iranian side, according to Iranian media, is going to be leading the delegation in the talks. Does that tell us anything useful about the extent of what Iran will be asking for here? Well, in terms of who's in charge, I think right now it's important to see the Iranian system as institutionalized and they rely on a sort of consensus-making decision process. So Ghalibaf is probably out there as a key figure because he's well known, he's a regime insider, he's conservative but also pragmatic and has close ties as a former member of the IRGC. So he brings together a lot of different elements from across the Iranian system. He will not make decisions on his own. He will take what's on the table back to Tehran and I think we will see a lot of back and forth. The state and health of the Supreme Leader is still very unknown; there are reports, of course, that he's incapacitated. But we should see this very much as a structural decision-making process rather than an individual that is in command and control. And what about Iran's proxies in the region? How much sway will Iran have over them and how they respond at this time? And we keep in mind of course Israel has said that it does not consider this to be a ceasefire on the Lebanon front. Yes, that's an interesting demand on Iran's side, looking to shield Hezbollah and the Houthis from further strikes. I think that demand is really unachievable and if Iran goes ahead and cuts a deal with President Trump and leaves the Hezbollah front vulnerable to further Israeli incursions and strikes, that will say a lot about the dynamics between Tehran and these proxies. Ultimately, I would imagine that the Islamic Republic will prioritize its own interests, but that is, you know, certainly going to be a sticking point and one that Netanyahu has already made clear is not connected to the process in Islamabad. Sanam, how much sway does Beijing have in Tehran right now? I think China has certainly played a very important behind-the-scenes role. I think that they pressured Iran or convinced Iran that as of right now, it has increased its leverage through the control it exerts over the Strait of Hormuz, and this was the right time to come to the negotiating table. So China also just vetoed a resolution at the Security Council and perhaps this was part of the quid pro quo. China would support the blocking of that resolution but would encourage Iran at least to come to the negotiating table. And we should see this as a first step. This is a pause; this is not a final settlement. We can all celebrate when this is done based on the terms and conditions of the deal. Sanam, thanks for the update. Appreciate it. Sanam Vakil, Middle East and North Africa program director joining us from Chatham House. Let's take a closer look at the outlook for oil as it plunges below $100 a barrel on the ceasefire—temporary as it might be, at least for now in the Middle East. That is next. This is Bloomberg.
