Prada Group CEO: Why Patience Beats Trends | Podcast | In Good Company

Norges Bank Investment Management(105.0K) ·39:19 ·2.9K views · 2026-04-08
Nicolai Tangen (CEO, Norges Bank Investment Management) Andrea Guerra (CEO, Prada Group)
Prada Group CEO Andrea Guerra argues that the luxury industry must return to its "old normal" of exclusivity and patient brand-building, prioritizing emotional storytelling and creative tension over chasing short-term trends.
TL;DR Prada Group CEO Andrea Guerra argues that the luxury industry must return to its "old normal" of exclusivity and patient brand-building, prioritizing emotional storytelling and creative tension over chasing short-term trends. By leveraging data-driven CRM and revitalizing...

TL;DR

Prada Group CEO Andrea Guerra argues that the luxury industry must return to its "old normal" of exclusivity and patient brand-building, prioritizing emotional storytelling and creative tension over chasing short-term trends. By leveraging data-driven CRM and revitalizing iconic brands like Miu Miu and Versace, the group aims to scale to a €10 billion ambition while maintaining a founder-led, "rebellious" cultural identity.

The Return to "Old Normal" in Luxury

  • Normalization of Growth: Following decades of unsustainable expansion, Guerra believes the industry is entering a period of "normalization" where success depends on returning to exclusivity, value, and selectivity.
  • Exclusivity over Expansion:
      • Store Strategy: Despite significant growth, Prada has only expanded from 170 to 176 stores in three years. Guerra prefers enlarging existing locations over opening new ones to maintain scarcity.
      • Hospitality-Driven Retail: The group is shifting retail spaces toward experience-based models, such as converting entire floors in Hong Kong into "apartments" for exclusive guest use.
  • The "Price" Fallacy: In true luxury, the goal is to sell an emotion or a dream so effectively that the consumer doesn't question the price. If price becomes the main topic of conversation, the brand has failed its storytelling mission.

Brand DNA and Creative Tension

  • Prada as Culture: Prada is defined as a "point of view" rooted in art, architecture, and literature. Guerra maintains that while the world and trends change, the brand's core opinion must remain constant.
  • Miu Miu’s Growth:
      • Inclusive Rebellion: Miu Miu is described as "rebellious" and "out of the fashion logic," yet inclusive across age groups (25–50).
      • Market Potential: While focused on women’s wear, Guerra sees significant room for growth compared to larger single-gender peers.
  • Creative Management: Winning brands require a "constant positive tension" between brand management and creative direction. Guerra emphasizes long-term commitments to creative directors over short-term, cyclical hires.

Strategic Acquisitions and Global Outlook

  • The Versace Turnaround:
      • The Rationale: Prada Group acquired Versace to bring the iconic Italian brand back to domestic ownership and leverage its roots in "glamour" and "pop" culture.
      • Long-Term Nurturing: Guerra acknowledges past mismanagement and intends to "plant seeds" and "nurture" the brand without expecting immediate, overnight success.
  • Scale and Protection: The group aims for a €10 billion revenue target, not to become a conglomerate like LVMH, but because scale provides protection in a volatile global market.
  • Regional Dynamics:
      • Europe: Currently acting as a "tourist market" under pressure, with Asian tourism at 50% of 2019 levels and American tourism declining due to geopolitical concerns.
      • China: Following a period of "digestion," the Chinese market is showing a new, slightly positive plateau.

Technology, Sustainability, and the Young Consumer

  • AI and Personalization: Prada’s primary use of Artificial Intelligence is within CRM (Customer Relationship Management) to match specific "drops" and messages to individual consumer data, significantly increasing conversion rates.
  • Sustainability as a Standard: For younger generations, sustainability is a non-negotiable baseline.
      • Re-Nylon: Prada’s transition to sustainable fibers is tied directly to ocean pollution education and specific price points designed to attract younger demographics.
  • Quiet Luxury: Guerra dismisses the trend of "quiet luxury," stating that the only metric that matters is brand "credibility" over time.

Leadership and Corporate Evolution

  • Resonant Leadership: Guerra defines his style as "convincing the mind and heart" that the team is on a shared journey, allowing individuals to express themselves fully.
  • The Power of Debate: Corporate decisions at Prada are reached through intense, often multi-day debates. Guerra calls this "upper compromise"—reaching the best decision rather than a middle-ground settlement.
  • Operational Efficiency:
      • Energy vs. Experience: Guerra believes "energy beats experience," though he uses his 25 years as a CEO to listen for what people *aren't* saying.
      • Anti-Bureaucracy: He has strictly limited budget presentations to 10 pages to eliminate "formal slide management."
"Luxury is exclusivity, is selectivity, is value, is dreams, is experience, is hospitality. This is what we have to do. And this is what it has always been." — Andrea Guerra
"It's risky to follow the trends. That's risky. You need to always in your brand... surprise you, comfort you, and we need to do something new together." — Andrea Guerra
"I still think that energy beats experience. And I want to prove it every day... if you don't have experience you cannot hold that position—I don't believe it." — Andrea Guerra
Hi everyone. Today I'm in Milan, the heart of Italian fashion, and I'm in extremely good company with Andrea Guerra, CEO of the Prada Group. Now, Andrea took over three years ago, stepped into the Prada shoes, and at that time the group has grown quarter by quarter. Miu Miu is arguably now the hottest brand in luxury, and you just bought Versace. So, warm welcome. Welcome, and happy you're here. Very good. Now, Prada is more than 100 years old. What's the DNA of Prada, in short? You know, Prada is a unique brand. It's a point of view. Prada is an opinion. Prada is culture. Prada is totally winded in certain cultural pillars such as art, architecture, literature, and it always has an opinion. And how do you see that in the brand? You can see that in the brand in any aspect, in any location, in anything we do. There is nothing which is not thought. There is nothing where you can spot details which are out of place. There is nothing that we do by chance and not linked to a thought and to an opinion about what's going on in the society. So what's constant and what is changing in the brand? Nothing is changing. Nothing is changing. Obviously, the world is changing. The trends are changing. Fashion is changing. But the brand is always that same thing. How is fashion changing now? How is the luxury industry changing? You know, I think this industry is upside down. What does that mean? This industry has gone through a couple of decades of constant growth. Let's go to any textbook; that's not possible. That's not feasible. And I think we were all happy. This industry has grown by three times, four times, five times in the last 10-15 years only. So, I think that now we have to go through a period of normalization. We have to go through a period where maybe we've been a little bit spoiled. So we have to go back to certain aspects of the business. So what's normalization? What's the new normal? I think it's the old normal. This is what we have to look for. It's not the new normal, it's the old normal. Luxury is exclusivity, is selectivity, is value, is dreams, is experience, is hospitality. This is what we have to do. And this is what it has always been. So how are you adapting to the new, the new old? You know, I can tell you that there are certain things which are very visible. We have gone through a refurbishing of a huge store in Hong Kong, where we would have followed our usual logic of one floor for men, one floor for women, one floor for accessories. Instead, we have decided to allow one entire floor to be an apartment, to be a place which is totally dressed by our creative directors. So it's a unique spot where you can spend time, where you can have friends for dinner, for lunch, or spend time for your birthday. And we're happy about that and we're happy to offer you very special and exclusive products. And this is the old normal. Right. You talk about change, but at the same time I hear you talk about the importance of patience. How does patience play in? You know, I think that the winning brands are those brands where there is a constant positive tension between brand management and creative direction. And this means patience. This means listening to each other. This means debating. This means taking the proper time to take decisions on how and where the brand is going and where creativity is going. And this is long term. It cannot be short term. In which brands in the world are you seeing that creative tension? I can talk about my brands. And we have it. We have it. We have long-term solutions. We don't have a creative director coming in and going out. We have long-term commitments. And that's the only thing that the consumer will pay in the long term. Then you can have a success here, a success there. But that's short term. Talking of creativity, you started Miu Miu from scratch, right, in '93? And it's an extraordinary brand growing very, very fast. Why is it so successful? You know, Miu Miu has... I always say Miu Miu is an inclusive brand. Miu Miu is women only. It's imprinted in a young lady, but it's inclusive. You don't feel wrong in wearing Miu Miu on a Monday morning or on a Saturday night. You don't feel wrong being 25 years old or 50 years old. You don't feel wrong with Miu Miu. You say it's rebellious. How? It's out of the schemes. It's out of the fashion logic. How large can a brand be and still be rebellious and out of the fashion? You know, I think it's... If we look to ladies-only brands, there are brands which are four to five times bigger than us. So I'm not alluding that we have to be there, but there is space to grow. What is important is that we have somehow attracted a lot of brands in our field. We don't have to move from our field. Typically, you see these three-year cycles. Do you think we'll see that with... You know, this is a five-year cycle, which has lasted longer than what we expected. And we need to be ready for normalization; we need to be ready for normal growth. And you know, you're never ready for that. But we have to be ready for that. We don't have to be complacent. It's a tough... we have to be pretty masochistic. What are the tools you use to be masochistic? You know, it's 'no'. My usual answer is 'no'. "We're offering you another store." "No." "We're offering you this opportunity." "No." So this is what we do. We had 170 stores three years ago. I think we have 176 today, with the kind of growth we had. So I prefer to enlarge certain stores, but I don't want new stores. In five years' time, maybe we will have 180 stores, 185 stores. But that's it. And that gives you the idea that we have not opened the gates to success. We've not gone into new categories. We've not opened to men. It's us. This is what we are. This is who we are. And these are the people we want to talk to. Tell me about emotions and dreams. It's all about emotions and dreams. If... you know, I'm always... and this is happening since I was selling white goods, can you imagine? Because I have known you for 20 years, and you have talked about this all the time. And this is it. If I'm able to tell you a story that makes sense, if I'm able to get your emotions going, it's much easier to have your hand going to the pocket and getting the wallet out. But I need to be absolutely credible and I have to be into the brand and I have to engage you. And this is everything we're trying to do. Where did you learn storytelling? You know, I think it's... I think that in management... Maybe you had a grandfather who was telling you stories when you were... If I take my father, my father probably was saying five words in a day. So it was not him as a storyteller. But I can tell you that there are two things in management which I particularly trust. One thing is that people and emotions are the vehicle to everything. The second is when you are able to manage the mix, your profit and loss is solid. And these two things go much together because when you're telling a story, you need to understand if you're going towards an enterprise or if you want to dream of 100,000 euros. And it's not anymore how you're dressed, how you are entering a store, or who you are because, I mean, tech has totally revolutionized the society and how people are coming into stores. Sometimes you can be really misinterpreting what's happening in the store right now. You know, I think there are two things. First of all, when people are asking me how are we using artificial intelligence, the main part we're using artificial intelligence is in our CRM. So, customer relationship management? Yes. We are working like hell, hours and hours, to really understand which message to which people and how to personalize that message to that person in that moment. And the more you're able to do that, the more business becomes hot. Okay, so I'm coming into your store, you know my background, you know what I bought with you. You are in that sustainable world. You are in digital, you don't have to think about it. Then if we go in government and politics, maybe yes. Moving on. Versace. Iconic. We feel that the two engines we had can take us somewhere close to 10 billion euros as an ambition. What's the DNA of Versace? Versace is something we like because, again, it was born in culture. Versace invented the great models. Versace invented music in the shows. So it's this spin, which is very different from our portfolio, but it's in our DNA that we love that everything goes and starts from culture. She is part of our team in giving an image and networking which we love. This is the first. It has been... has been a little bit mismanaged as a brand, not as a business, and we're ready to go. And we feel that we know how to do a brand business. We are patient. We don't think that we have to be successful tomorrow morning, but we need to plant seeds, we need to nurture, we need to have the right people in place, and the team is ready to go. Each one of us is spending—I don't want to say money, could be money, could be time—in so many different things we love. This industry has lost one consumer out of five in the last four years, three years. It's a lot, huh? I mean, how are the dynamics in China changing? I don't know if they are changing. And I would say that even because of the wars, Europe today is the animal or is the region that is digesting the great growth it had. Europe is by definition a tourist market. And today, I mean, Americans are not coming because they think the war is in Europe. Asians cannot come because, I mean, it's so difficult today to fly over East-West. BREAKING: JD Vance Speaks With Press After Iran Says US Violated Ceasefire—Closes Strait Of Hormuz

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance confirmed a tenuous ceasefire is in effect with Iran, clarifying that upcoming negotiations in Islamabad will prioritize the permanent reopening of the Strait of Hormuz and the removal of nuclear fuel, while explicitly stating that Lebanon was never included in the current truce.

Diplomatic Mission to Hungary

  • Counteracting EU Influence: Vance stated the primary purpose of his trip was to counteract "propaganda" from the European Union bureaucracy in Brussels.
      • Election Interference: He accused the EU of attempting to impose costs on the Hungarian people to sway election results, calling the practice "absurd."
  • Support for Viktor Orbán: Vance reaffirmed that both he and President Trump give their "full support" to Prime Minister Orbán and the Hungarian people.

Clarifying the Iran Ceasefire Framework

  • The "Three Proposals" Problem: Vance warned the public against "maximalist" or fake proposals circulating in the media.
      • Rejected Drafts: One ten-point proposal was reportedly written by AI (ChatGPT) and immediately rejected by U.S. negotiators Steve Witkoff and Jared Kushner.
      • Legitimate Framework: The official proposal, referenced by President Trump, was developed through back-and-forth negotiations involving U.S., Iranian, and Pakistani officials.
  • Strait of Hormuz Status: Vance reported that while the waterway is starting to reopen and oil prices are stabilizing, the U.S. will only abide by ceasefire terms if the Straits remain open for traffic.
  • Media Criticism: Vance criticized major outlets like the *New York Times* and *CNN* for reporting "fringe" Iranian leaks as official negotiating positions.

Regional Conflicts and Lebanon

  • Lebanon Exclusion: Vance clarified a "legitimate misunderstanding" regarding the scope of the ceasefire. He stated the U.S. never promised to include Lebanon in the truce; the agreement is focused strictly on Iran and U.S. allies (Israel and Gulf Arab states).
  • Israeli Posture: While not a condition of the ceasefire, Vance noted that Israel has offered to "check themselves" regarding strikes in Lebanon to facilitate the success of the broader negotiation.
  • Negotiation Team: Vance credited Steve Witkoff and Jared Kushner as the primary "on the ground" negotiators, supported by a broader team including Pete Hegseth and Marco Rubio.

Nuclear Objectives and Leverage

  • Primary Demands: The U.S. objective remains preventing Iran from obtaining a nuclear weapon. Specifically, the U.S. is demanding that Iran "give up the nuclear fuel."
  • Negotiating Carrots and Sticks:
      • Economic Incentives: Sanctions relief and economic partnerships are on the table, but only if Iran makes firm commitments to end nuclear development.
      • Military Leverage: Vance defended the President’s aggressive rhetoric, stating that if Iran breaks the bargain, they will face "serious consequences" from the U.S. military.
  • Response to Mohammad Ghalibaf: Addressing the Iranian Parliament Speaker’s complaints that three points of the plan were violated, Vance argued that if only three points are in dispute, it suggests there is broad agreement on the remaining points of the 15-point framework.
